The security guard’s hand pushed against my back, steering me through the maze-like corridors hidden behind the casino. Each step felt heavier than the last, my heartbeat a percussion instrument drowning out the muffled slot machine symphonies beyond the walls. “In here,” he said, opening a door that seemed to swallow light rather than emit it. I entered, my fingers fidgeting with the hem of my blouse. The room smelled of stale coffee and something sharper—fear, perhaps. My fear. “Sit.” The word sliced through the dim air. I lowered myself into the chair opposite the desk, the vinyl creaking beneath me like a confession. Behind the desk sat a woman whose posture suggested she had steel for a spine. Veronica. The name badge on her tailored blazer caught what little light existed in the room, winking at me mockingly. “Ms. Rahel Vega,” she said, not a question but an accusation. “I know who you are. You own a spiritual supply shop called Empowering Tarot.” “Yes,” I whispered, my voice betraying me. I cleared my throat. “That’s me.” “Do you know why you’re here?” Veronica’s eyes were calculating, measuring the tremor in my hands, the sweat beading at my hairline. I considered lying, but falsehood had never sat well with me. “I think so.” Veronica’s fingers began a rhythmic tap-tap-tap against her desk. The sound reminded me of rain on a tin roof, of waiting for storms to pass. But this storm was just beginning. “You had quite the winning streak tonight,” she said, her voice like silk over sandpaper. “Remarkable, really. Some might say… too remarkable.” My throat constricted. “I was lucky.” “Luck.” The word hung between us, suspended in the stagnant air. “We don’t believe in luck here, Ms. Vega. We believe in statistics, probabilities… and surveillance.” Her hand moved toward a remote control resting beside a computer monitor angled away from my view. Her crimson nails scraped against the plastic, the sound sending shivers down my spine. “I wasn’t—” I started, but she raised her other hand, silencing me. “Save it,” Veronica said, her gaze piercing through me like a knife through gauze. “The cameras never lie, even when people do.” I felt my lungs collapse, imaginary walls pressing in from all sides. What would they see? Had my whispered conversations with empty air been caught on camera? Would they notice the slight pause before each winning hand, the moment when guidance came from voices only I could hear? “Please,” I said, unsure what I was pleading for. Mercy? Understanding? The impossible chance she might believe that sometimes the dead whisper winning combinations to the living? Veronica’s lips curled into something too sharp to be a smile. “Let’s watch together, shall we? I find footage like this… educational.” Her finger hovered above the play button, the tap-tap-tapping momentarily suspended. In that breathless moment, I wondered if compassion could save me, if my earnest explanations might penetrate her professional armor. But her eyes revealed nothing but cold determination as she leaned forward, remote in hand, prepared to expose what she believed was my deception. “I think you’ll find this illuminating,” she said, her voice dropping to a whisper that felt more threatening than any shout. I gripped the edges of my seat, knuckles whitening, waiting for my secrets to play out on the screen before us. Then a shimmer – like heat rising from pavement – appeared behind Veronica’s rigid shoulders. The air condensed, particles gathering into the familiar silhouette of Auntie, her spectral form draped in flowing fabrics that moved without the aid of any breeze. A jolt of recognition shot through me, and I stifled a gasp. “Something wrong, Ms. Vega?” Veronica asked, misreading my expression. “No, I—” I stammered, trying desperately not to stare at Auntie, who was now wiggling her translucent eyebrows comically. Auntie winked at me, her eyes twinkling with mischief I’d come to recognize as her signature look before chaos ensued. She raised a finger to her lips, then made an exaggerated pantomime of cutting wires with spectral scissors. “I’m just anxious to see what you think you’ve found,” I said, my voice steadier than I felt as Auntie circled behind Veronica, giving me looks that made clear that she had some kind of a plan. Veronica narrowed her eyes. “Anxiety is the natural response to guilt.” “Or to being falsely accused,” I countered, drawing confidence from Auntie’s presence. The spirit now stood directly behind Veronica, making calming gestures, drawing her hands downward in the universal sign for ‘relax.’ The air in the room shifted subtly – a drop in temperature, a change in pressure – nothing Veronica would consciously notice, but I felt it in my bones. Auntie tapped her wrist, then formed a circle with her thumb and forefinger. Everything will be okay. “Let’s get this over with,” Veronica said, pressing the play button with triumphant finality. The screen remained black. She pressed again, harder this time. Nothing. “What the—” Veronica muttered, turning the remote over to check the batteries. I fought to keep my expression neutral, even as Auntie smiled satisfied over the victory. The tension crescendoed as Veronica jabbed repeatedly at the remote, each failed attempt darkening her expression. I could almost hear the tick-tick-tick of her patience unwinding. The door burst open. “Ms. Veronica!” A young man in a casino uniform stood there, breathing heavily. “There’s an urgent call for you on line one. They say it’s about a major security breach at the south entrance.” “Now?” Veronica snapped, slapping the remote down. “I’m in the middle of something important.” “They insisted it couldn’t wait. Said it was the owner himself.” Auntie gave me a conspiratorial wink and a thumbs-up. Veronica exhaled sharply through her nostrils. “Fine.” She pointed at me, her nail a crimson dagger. “Don’t. Move. I’ll be back in two minutes, and we will finish this.” “Of course,” I said, folding my hands in my lap, the picture of compliance. She stalked toward the door, turning back one last time. “This doesn’t change anything. The evidence is quite damning.” After she left, Auntie’s form began to fade, her outline blurring at the edges like watercolors bleeding into paper. Her expression remained triumphant, her eyes sparkling with satisfaction as she dissipated into the shadows, leaving me alone with the blank screen and the faint scent of lilacs – Auntie’s perfume – hanging in the air. The moment the door clicked shut, silence descended like a heavy blanket. I could hear the hum of the overhead lights, the soft whir of the computer, and my own shallow breathing. My thoughts raced like frightened birds trapped in a too-small cage. What was on that footage? How much had they seen? The money I’d pocketed felt like burning coals in my purse. “You’re in quite the predicament now, aren’t you?” I nearly jumped out of my skin. Mister B. had materialized in the chair beside me, his spectral form crisp and clear despite having no physical substance. “What am I going to do?” I whispered, my fingers digging into the armrests. “She has evidence. Real evidence.” “Perhaps,” Mister B. said, his voice carrying the rasp of someone who had smoked too much in life. “But evidence only matters if you’re still here when she returns to show it.” “You want me to run?” The idea seemed absurd, yet my heart leapt at the possibility. “Listen carefully, Rahel,” he leaned closer, his form wavering slightly as he did. “The woman will return in less than a minute. There’s a service corridor three doors down on the right. It leads past the kitchens to the emergency exit near the east parking lot.” I shook my head. “I can’t just—” “You can and you will,” he cut me off, his tone brooking no argument. “This isn’t the time for your characteristic hesitation. You think they’ll be lenient because you look frightened? Because you say pretty words? These people break fingers over unpaid debts.” “But running makes me look guilty,” I protested, though my resolve was weakening by the second. Mister B.’s form flickered impatiently. “You are guilty. Now get up. Walk, don’t run. Look like you belong. And whatever you do, don’t look back.” My heart hammered against my ribs like a wild thing seeking escape. Could I really do this? Just walk out? What about consequences? What about— “Now, Rahel!” Mister B. hissed, his form surging toward me with sudden intensity that made the air temperature drop several degrees. That did it. I rose from my chair, my legs trembling but holding. Every instinct screamed to stay put, to explain, to apologize. “Good,” Mister B. nodded approvingly. “Head high. Confidence, not arrogance. You’ve done nothing wrong according to anyone who matters.” Ensure your favorite authors get the support they deserve. Read this novel on Royal Road. I took a steadying breath and moved toward the door, each step feeling like I was walking through molasses. My fingertips buzzed with adrenaline as I grasped the cool metal handle. “She’ll be coming back up the main hallway,” Mister B. whispered, now hovering near my shoulder. “Turn right immediately.” I pulled the door open just enough to slip through, my senses heightened to painful clarity. The carpet seemed too loud beneath my feet, the dim hallway lights too bright. I could hear my own pulse in my ears, drowning out everything but Mister B.’s urgent whispers guiding me toward escape. The corridor stretched before me, empty but foreboding. Each recessed light cast pools of amber that barely penetrated the gloom between them. I moved like a thief through a museum, hugging the wall. “Left at the next junction,” Mister B. instructed, his translucent form gliding just ahead. “Security office is ahead on the right—don’t even glance that way.” My shoes made soft whispers against the plush carpet. I tried to match my breathing to my footsteps—quiet, measured, invisible. “They’re watching for someone running,” Mister B. said. “Walk like you belong. Like you’ve been sent on an errand.” A distant voice echoed down a connecting hallway. I froze. “Keep moving,” Mister B. growled. “Stopping makes you suspicious.” I forced my leaden feet forward, past the security office where shadows moved behind frosted glass. My fingers traced the textured wallpaper as I went, seeking something solid in a world gone liquid with fear. “Through those double doors,” Mister B. pointed. “Service corridor beyond. Quick now.” The doors opened with a soft hiss that sounded like thunder to my overwrought nerves. “Nervous people look down,” Mister B. reminded me. “Keep your chin up.” “I can’t breathe,” I whispered. “You’re doing fine. Three more turns and we’re at the emergency exit.” Fluorescent lighting replaced the warm glow of the main corridors. We were in the staff areas now—stark white walls, linoleum floors that amplified every footfall. “Someone’s coming,” Mister B. warned. A maintenance worker rounded the corner ahead, pushing a cart of cleaning supplies. My throat constricted. “Smile and nod,” Mister B. instructed. I did. The worker barely glanced up, returning my nod with disinterest before continuing on his way. “Last turn,” Mister B. said as we approached a T-junction. “Right, then straight to the end.” The emergency exit loomed ahead, its red sign casting a bloody glow onto the institutional white walls. I approached it with mounting dread, each step heavier than the last. “It’s alarmed,” I whispered, staring at the push bar across the door and the small warning sign beside it. “Of course it’s alarmed,” Mister B. replied, his patience wearing thin. “That’s the point.” My hand hovered over the bar, trembling. “They’ll know it was me.” “They already suspect you of theft. What’s a fire alarm compared to that?” I hesitated, fingers inches from the cool metal. “There has to be another way.” “There isn’t,” Mister B. said, his voice surprisingly gentle now. “Sometimes the only way out is through the fire, Rahel.” Behind us, a door slammed. Voices. Coming closer. My hand still wouldn’t move. “You’ve spent your whole life afraid to set off alarms,” Mister B. said. “Look where it’s gotten you.” The truth of his words stung more than any rebuke. I pressed my palm against the bar, feeling resistance, the weight of consequence. “What happens after?” I whispered. “Freedom happens,” Mister B. replied. “But only if you push.” I closed my eyes, drew a breath deep into my lungs, and shoved with all my might against the bar. The alarm shrieked to life, a banshee wail that ripped through the casino’s hushed atmosphere. I bolted through the door, the cold night air slapping me awake like an ice bath. The stark difference between the casino’s artificial warmth and the biting autumn chill made me gasp, but I didn’t slow down. “Left! Now right at the dumpsters!” Mister B. commanded, his voice somehow cutting through the din of the alarm. My legs pumped beneath me, high heels abandoned somewhere in the first twenty steps, stockinged feet barely registering the rough pavement. Each breath tore at my throat, hot and painful. Behind me, shouts erupted from the building—muffled at first, then clearer as other doors opened. “She’s headed for the main road!” A man’s voice, authoritative and angry. The bus stop was on the other side of the parking lot. My lungs already burned. “They’re following,” I panted, not daring to look back. “And you’re faster,” Mister B. insisted. “You’ve always been faster than you believed.” The casino’s floodlights didn’t reach this far into the parking lot. Darkness swallowed me as I darted between parked cars, crouching low, the hard-won money weighing heavy in my purse with each frantic step. “Will I make it?” I gasped, heart hammering against my ribs. “You will if you stop wasting breath on questions,” Mister B. replied. The alarm’s wail grew fainter with distance. I emerged onto the sidewalk, streetlights casting pools of sickly yellow glow every fifty feet. Between them, shadow. I ran through light, through dark, through light again. Each illumination felt like exposure, each shadow like temporary salvation. The bus stop appeared ahead—a simple metal bench beneath a plexiglass shelter. Empty. No bus in sight. “Did we miss it?” I wheezed, stumbling the final yards to the shelter. “Look,” said Mister B., somehow directing my gaze to the right. There it was—the number 42 night bus, headlights cutting through the darkness. My salvation, if only it would stop. I staggered to the curb, waving my arms frantically, my shadow dancing grotesquely behind me. The bus seemed to slow, but was it stopping? Would the driver see a disheveled woman, barefoot and wild-eyed, and decide to keep going? “Please,” I whispered, then louder: “PLEASE!” The bus’s brakes hissed. It pulled alongside the curb, doors folding open with a pneumatic sigh. I lurched forward, grabbing the handrail and pulling myself up the steps, collapsing against the fare box. My breath came in ragged gasps, each one burning like fire in my chest. “You got somewhere to be in such a hurry?” the driver asked, eyebrows raised. I fumbled in my purse, past the casino chips, finding my transit card. My hands shook so badly I could barely tap it against the reader. “Home,” I managed, the word emerging as little more than a croak. “Just… home.” The doors closed behind me with finality. Through the windows, I could see two security guards appear at the corner, scanning the street frantically. The bus pulled away from the curb, engine growling, carrying me into the night and away from my pursuers. I collapsed into the first empty seat, my legs finally giving out beneath me. The fluorescent lights cast a sickly pallor over the nearly empty bus, revealing the tremor in my hands as they clutched my purse against my chest. “What did you do?” I hissed under my breath, eyes darting to make sure no one sat close enough to hear. “What did all of you do? I was supposed to leave quietly with my winnings, not flee like a criminal!” I could feel them there—Auntie, Ma, Mister B., Grandpa—hovering at the edges of my awareness, silent but present. Their collective silence only fueled my mounting anger. “Veronica had footage,” I continued, my voice rising slightly. “Evidence that could have—that could still—get me arrested. And your brilliant plan was to set off the fire alarm? To make me run?” A middle-aged woman sitting across the aisle shifted uncomfortably, deliberately looking out the window. “Don’t pretend you can’t hear me,” I said, glaring at the empty space beside me. “You promised to help me, not turn me into a fugitive. How am I supposed to face any of this now?” In the rearview mirror, I caught the bus driver’s eyes flicking toward me, then quickly back to the road. His mouth tightened into a thin line as I continued my one-sided argument. “I trusted you,” I whispered harshly, tears threatening to spill. “I put everything on the line, and for what? So you could have your fun watching me scramble through the night like some desperate—” The bus lurched to a stop, and the driver cleared his throat pointedly. “Everything okay back there, miss?” Heat crept up my neck, spreading across my cheeks. The few other passengers were now openly staring, their faces a mixture of concern and discomfort. “Fine,” I said, forcing a weak smile. “Just… rehearsing for a play.” The driver held my gaze in the mirror for a beat too long, unconvinced. “Right. Well, keep it down a bit. Disturbing the other passengers.” I nodded, shrinking into my seat, mortification mingling with my fear and anger. As the bus resumed its journey through the dark streets, I pressed my forehead against the cool glass of the window, watching the city lights blur through unshed tears. “You’ve made me look crazy,” I murmured, barely audible now. “But I suppose that’s fitting. Only a crazy person would listen to ghosts in the first place.” Mister B.’s reflection materialized in the window beside me, superimposed over the passing streetlights. His expression was maddeningly calm, a stark contrast to the storm raging inside me. “There was nothing unplanned about tonight, my dear,” he said, his voice a smooth, unhurried baritone. “Quite the contrary. Everything unfolded precisely as it needed to.” I clenched my jaw. “Being chased out of a casino by security was part of your grand design?” “The universe rarely offers straight paths,” he replied, adjusting his spectral bow tie with maddening composure. “Sometimes the route to success requires… creative navigation.” “Creative navigation,” I echoed bitterly. “Is that what we’re calling it?” Mister B.’s eyes flickered with something—perhaps amusement, perhaps pride. “You adapted beautifully. That’s the mark of a survivor.” The bus hit a pothole, and I clutched my purse tighter, feeling the reassuring weight of the casino chips within. My fingers trembled, adrenaline still coursing through my veins. “They’ll be looking for me,” I whispered. Grandpa’s presence settled into the empty seat beside me, his weathered features softening as he regarded me with familiar affection. “Look at what you’ve got clutched in your hands, Rachel-girl,” he said, nodding toward my purse. His voice carried the same comforting timbre it had when I was a child scared of thunderstorms. “Not bad for a night’s work, is it?” “It won’t matter if I’m arrested for theft.” Grandpa chuckled, the sound like autumn leaves rustling. “They’ve got bigger fish to fry than chasing down a small-timer who got lucky. Besides,” he tapped his temple with a translucent finger, “the house always expects to lose sometimes. It’s built into their calculations.” “The old man is right,” Mister B. interjected. “And what’s more, you’ve learned something invaluable tonight.” I raised an eyebrow. “That I should never listen to spirits with questionable moral compasses?” “That you possess more courage than you give yourself credit for,” he corrected, his usual impatience softening. “When the moment demanded action, you didn’t hesitate.” Grandpa reached out as if to pat my hand, his touch nothing more than a cool breeze against my skin. “We never doubted you for a second, sweetheart. Not for one second.” The driver glanced back at me in the rearview mirror again, concern etched in the creases around his eyes. I realized I’d been nodding, responding to voices only I could hear. I looked away quickly, but the weight of Grandpa’s pride and Mister B.’s quiet confidence lingered, gradually tempering the chaos of my emotions. The bus lurched to a stop, throwing me forward. Outside the window, streetlights flickered like dying stars against the night sky. My hands still trembled as I clutched the backpack containing my winnings. “Maybe they’re right,” I whispered to myself, low enough that the driver wouldn’t hear. “Maybe I did get away with it.” But even as relief washed over me in tentative waves, something dark and jagged remained lodged in my chest. A splinter of doubt. Of fear. “You’re overthinking again,” Auntie materialized beside me, her form wavering like heat rising from summer pavement. “Always making mountains of molehills.” I pressed my forehead against the cool glass. “A security breach at a casino is hardly a molehill.” “And yet here you sit, free as a bird.” Her laughter swirled around me like perfume. “With enough money to make some real changes.” The thought sent a jolt through me. Changes. Possibilities. For the first time in years, I had options beyond scraping by. “I could afford staying some extra months in my apartment,” I murmured. “You could do more than that,” Mister B. appeared across the aisle, his spectral form strangely dignified against the grimy bus interior. “You could move forward with your life. Finally.” The driver’s eyes flicked to the rearview mirror again. I caught his gaze this time and offered a weak smile, which he did not return. “They’ll come looking for me.” The words tasted like ash in my mouth. “Veronica knows my name, and the name of my store.” Grandpa materialized, sitting in front of me but turned backward. “Casinos deal with thousands of guests every day. You are just one more in the crowd.” “Unless…” I bit my lip, the possibilities unfurling like a map of dangerous terrain. “Unless I run. Really run. Start somewhere new.” The spirits exchanged glances—a conspiracy of the dead conferring over the fate of the living. “Now you’re thinking,” Auntie beamed. “That little apartment of yours was never meant to be permanent anyway.” I closed my eyes, imagining freedom. A clean slate. But beneath that fantasy lurked the shadow of what I was becoming—a person who runs, who takes what isn’t hers, who consorts with spirits instead of people. “Is this who I am now?” The question escaped before I could catch it. “You are who you’ve always been,” Mister B. said, his voice like stone worn smooth by time. “Someone who survives. Someone who adapts.” The driver called out my stop, though I hadn’t requested it. Perhaps he was eager to be rid of the woman muttering to herself in the back of his bus. As I stood to leave, Grandpa’s voice followed me down the aisle. “The world was never built for people like us, Rahel. We make our own way through it.”

  [Part 7] - Vol. 1 Ch. 7: Better Watch Your Back

My chest tightened as the bus pulled away, its red taillights diminishing until they were swallowed by the darkness. The engine’s rumble faded, leaving only the whisper of wind through dead leaves and the thundering of my own pulse in my ears. I was alone. I closed my eyes and counted backward from ten, a childish ritual that had never quite lost its power. The images remained—the dealer’s fingers splayed across green felt, cards turning with mechanical precision, the tower of chips that had grown before me like something alive and hungry. Then the man in the suit appearing at my shoulder, his voice soft but edged with steel. His hand on my arm. The way the room had seemed to narrow, faces turning toward me with sudden interest. My hands trembled as I opened them. The night air turned my breath to ghost-shapes that dissolved before my eyes. I needed to think, to plan my next move. The money in my pocket felt impossibly heavy. Footsteps broke the silence—deliberate and unhurried, coming from the curve in the road. I stiffened, my body primed for flight. A figure emerged from the darkness, tall and narrow-shouldered, face half-hidden beneath the brim of a worn baseball cap. “Rough night?” His voice was like gravel wrapped in velvet. I said nothing, measuring the distance between us and calculating how quickly I could disappear. “The casino can do that to people.” He stopped at the edge of the lamplight, respectfully distant. “Seen it a hundred times.” My throat dried. “What makes you think I was at the casino?” He gestured vaguely toward the departing bus. “That route only comes from the casino or the truck stop, and you don’t look like you’ve been loading freight.” A pause. “Plus, you’ve got that look.” “What look?” The words came out sharper than I intended. “Like you’ve seen something you weren’t supposed to.” He stepped forward, and light slid across his face. He was older than I’d thought, lines etched deep around his eyes and mouth. Not handsome, but interesting—the face of someone with stories locked behind his teeth. “Name’s Jack.” I didn’t offer mine in return. “I should go.” “Where to?” „Home.“ Jack gestured to the bench. “Mind if I sit? Been on my feet all day.” I hesitated, then nodded curtly, shifting to the far end. He settled down with a sigh that seemed to carry the weight of years. “You don’t have to tell me anything,” he said, staring out at the darkness. “But sometimes it helps to talk to someone who doesn’t matter.” The simplicity of his statement caught me off guard. He was right—he didn’t matter. Tomorrow, I’d be gone, and he’d just be a strange footnote in this disastrous night. What harm could there be in unloading some of this pressure building in my chest? “I was winning,” I said finally. “A lot.” He nodded, unsurprised. “That’ll do it.” “Have you ever had one of those nights where everything just… works? Where you can see the patterns, feel the rhythm of the game?” My fingers traced circles on the bench between us. “That was tonight. I couldn’t lose if I tried. It was like the cards were speaking to me.” “And they noticed,” Jack said. Not a question. “A security guard approached me. Said Ms. Veronica wanted to speak with me in her office.” I swallowed hard. “There was something in her eyes—something cold. I’ve seen that look before. It doesn’t end well.” “So you ran.” “The situation was somewhat more complicated, but, essentially, yes.” Jack was quiet for a moment. “Smart move.” “Was it?” I looked at him directly now. “I don’t even know what I did wrong. I wasn’t counting cards. I’m not that good.” “Doesn’t matter.” Jack leaned forward, elbows on his knees. “You were winning too much, too consistently. Casino doesn’t like statistical anomalies. Makes them nervous.” The wind picked up, sending a shiver through me that had nothing to do with the cold. “What would they have done? If I’d stayed there?“ Jack didn’t answer immediately. His gaze was fixed on something distant, as though he were seeing beyond the darkness to something only he could perceive. When he looked back at me, his eyes were sharper somehow, more focused. Why wouldn’t he go?” My mouth had gone dry. “And then?” “Then nothing.” Jack spread his hands. “A few days later, he disappeared.” “You mean…?” I couldn’t finish the thought. “I mean he disappeared. Bank accounts never accessed again.” Jack’s voice had dropped to a near-whisper, forcing me to strain to hear him. “I guess he went back for another fortunate night and that wasn’t the best idea he ever had.” The night seemed to press closer around us. The yellow lamp buzzed overhead, its light suddenly insufficient against the weight of darkness. “You reported it to the police,” I said. “Of course. They investigated. Casino provided security footage showing Mark leaving the building at 2:17 a.m., walking to his car, driving away.“ Jack’s lip curled slightly. “Very helpful, they were. Concerned, even. Offered a reward for information.” “But you don’t believe it.” “The footage showed him getting into his car and driving away. The same car that was found in their parking lot.” Jack shrugged. “Police said he must have returned later. They’d scanned my ID. “You see my concern,” he said, reading my expression. The wind whispered through the dead grass beyond the road, a sound like secrets being passed between conspirators. The trees at the edge of the road swayed and groaned, branches scraping against each other like arthritic fingers. I watched Jack walk away, his figure gradually absorbed by the darkness until only the sound of his footsteps remained, and then even those faded to nothing. I sat alone, my thoughts a turbulent river. Part of me wanted to dismiss Jack’s warnings as the rambling of a conspiracy theorist or perhaps a grieving friend unable to accept a simpler truth. Yet something in his steady gaze, the measured cadence of his words, had struck a chord of truth that resonated within me. I stood, muscles stiff from sitting in the cold. The night stretched before me, vast and uncertain. A distant rumble of thunder rolled across the sky. The air had grown thick with the promise of rain, heavy with portent. I wondered, as the first drops of rain began to fall, if I would ever feel safe again. If this night marked not just a windfall but a watershed—the moment my life diverged permanently from its expected course. The thought should have terrified me, and part of it did. But beneath the fear lurked something else, something I was reluctant to acknowledge even to myself: a thrill. The same electric current I’d felt when the cards had fallen just right, when I’d known, somehow, exactly what would happen next. The night swallowed me, rain washing away my footprints almost as soon as they formed. Behind me, the bus stop receded into memory, its single light a dim star drowning in the darkness. Before me lay only questions without answers, and the strange, terrible freedom of having nothing left to lose. Continue the story here: https://www.patreon.com/RahelVega

  12. Elara Tideborn and the Rusty Dagger

  13. Noxborne
  Tác giả: Gware
  Tổng chương: 34
  Ngày cập nhật: N/A
  URL: https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/149931/noxborne
▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓

  Mô tả: Victor Hale studied fear for eight years. Now fear is all that keeps him alive. When Earth’s System Integration awakens a dormant trait inside him, Victor becomes Noxborne, a species fueled by terror. The power comes with an unending hunger. As his transformation accelerates, he must decide: feed on the fear of others or starve. With new phases approaching and deadlier threats emerging, Victor walks a razor’s edge between humanity and predation. He can choose who he feeds on. He just can’t choose what he’s becoming. A dark, psychological progression story loosely inspired by the boogeyman myth. Victor doesn’t haunt closets. He consumes fear itself, choosing when to be the monster and trying to stay human at least mentally.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Chapter 33: Shadow Sparring
  https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/149931/noxborne/chapter/3203517/chapter-33-shadow-sparring
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Jennifer’s breathing had settled into the deep rhythm of exhaustion hours ago. Victor watched her from the living room doorway, his enhanced perception tracking the steady rise and fall of her chest, the complete stillness that came from a body pushed past its limits. She’d collapsed onto his bed fully clothed except for her boots, and hadn’t stirred since. Maya paced the apartment’s narrow living room for the fifth time in as many minutes. Her footsteps carried a tension that Victor’s Life Sense read as pure restless energy, adrenaline still flooding her system despite the late hour. Every time she reached the window, she’d pause, stare out at Havenport’s transformed skyline, then resume pacing. Victor understood the feeling. His Dread Reservoir sat at full capacity, the feeding from last night still sustaining him in ways that made sleep feel optional. “I can’t sleep.” Maya stopped pacing and turned to face him. “And honestly, I don’t want to. I’ve been thinking about Battle Sense all day.” Victor moved away from the doorway. “What about it?” “It’s still Rank 1.” Maya’s hand found her axe handle at her belt. “The System says skills rank up through intensive use and understanding. The more I push it, the faster it evolves.” Her voice carried determination. “I felt the difference it made today against those Market enforcers. But it’s still crude. If I can rank it up to 2 or 3, imagine what it could tell me.” The logic was sound. Victor had noticed the same pattern with his own abilities. Shadow Stalker had become smoother with use. Dread Weaving responded faster the more he practiced. “You need intensive training.” The words came easier than expected. “Real application under pressure. Pushing the ability’s limits to force evolution.” Maya met his eyes, hope cutting through the frustration. “Will you help me? Work with me until I understand more details of what Battle Sense can do?” Victor hesitated. The protective instinct that had settled into his chest flared immediately, the same fierce need to shield Sarah from danger, to check Maya’s and Jennifer’s position during combat. But something else stirred beneath the protectiveness. The world ended four days ago. Monsters claimed territory. Slavers hunted survivors. Phase Two had just begun, and dangers multiplied faster than he could track. If he wasn’t there when they needed him, what then? “Victor?” Maya’s voice pulled him back. “I need you both strong May.” The words came out faster than he intended. “Strong enough to survive without me. Strong enough that if we get separated or I’m not there…” He met her eyes directly. The moonlight caught her face, revealing past exhaustion and armor to something he’d overlooked. Maya was fierce. Beautiful in a way that had nothing to do with softness and everything to do with the resolve in her eyes. She wasn’t asking for protection; she was demanding to become dangerous. “You and Jen,” he continued quietly. “You’re both mine to protect. But this world won’t let me be everywhere. I need to know you can stand without me.” Maya’s breath caught. “Then help me. Make me strong enough that you don’t have to worry. Make me someone who can protect Jennifer when you can’t.” Victor’s chest tightened. “That’s exactly what I plan to do.” “There’s an abandoned parking lot one block north.” Victor had mapped every structure within Life Sense range. “Open space, good sightlines. We can work there.” Maya grabbed her axe without hesitation. “Let’s go.” They left a note for Jennifer and descended the building’s stairwell, Shadow Stalker rendering their footsteps silent. The streets felt different at night. Havenport had developed nocturnal rhythms over four days of Integration, creatures and survivors alike adapting to patterns that hadn’t existed before. The parking lot emerged from the urban darkness like a stage waiting for performers. Three stories of a ruined office building provided a vertical element on the eastern edge. Concrete stretched open and clear for maybe fifty yards. Shadows pooled thick along the building’s base while moonlight caught the lot’s center. Victor’s Life Sense swept the perimeter. No threats within seventy-six feet. They were alone. Maya drew her fire axe and settled into a ready stance. “Don’t go easy on me. Battle Sense won’t rank up from easy fights. I need real pressure, real complexity.” Victor’s hands found his Shadow Fangs. “Then we push it. Close your eyes.” Maya hesitated but complied. Her stance tightened fractionally. Victor circled slowly. “What does Battle Sense tell you now?” “You’re moving clockwise. Speed is casual, not aggressive.” Maya’s head tracked his position despite closed eyes. “Your weight distribution is even. No immediate attack intent. There’s a threat signature, but it’s low intensity.” “Good.” Victor stopped moving. “Now I’m going to attack. Tell me what changes.” Shifting his weight forward, muscles coiling for a lunge he had no intention of completing. Maya’s reaction was immediate. Her eyes snapped open, and she spun to face him directly, axe coming up. “The threat spiked. Your whole signature changed from neutral to aggressive.” The words came fast, excited. “I felt it before you moved.” “That’s the ability working. Now we make it work harder.” They sparred for twenty minutes, Victor gradually increasing speed and complexity. Each exchange taught Maya something new about interpreting the information her abilities provided. By the fifteenth engagement, she was catching his feints without hesitation, her axe finding his daggers with precision that suggested real understanding. An idea crystallized during their next exchange. Victor created a dagger and threw it past her head, maintaining its form as it embedded in concrete behind her position. “I want you to track this even though you can’t see it now.” Maya turned her head. “I can feel it there. Like a small pressure point behind me.” She paused. “The signature is different from when you hold a normal weapon.” Victor focused on the embedded dagger, trying to explain the difference through Life Sense. Except his perception suddenly split, making his stomach lurch. He was seeing Maya from his normal position, fifteen feet in front of her. But at the same time, he saw her from behind, the perspective originating from where the shadow dagger was embedded. His knees buckled. The sensation of being in two places at once crashed through his awareness with enough force to make his head throb. “What just happened?” Maya moved toward him, concern replacing concentration. “Your eyes went completely black. No silver pupils, nothing.” Stolen content warning: this tale belongs on Royal Road. Report any occurrences elsewhere. Victor blinked, and the double vision collapsed back to normal. “I saw through the shadow. When I focused on the dagger, I could see you from its perspective. From behind.” Maya’s expression shifted to fascination. “Battle Sense just went haywire. You felt split. Like you were in two places simultaneously, can you do it again?” Victor created another construct and tossed it to his right. Then he closed his physical eyes and reached for the connection he’d accidentally discovered. The shadow responded as if it had been waiting. His perception flowed through the construct. Suddenly, he was seeing the parking lot from the knife’s position, looking back at himself with eyes closed, and Maya was watching him with rapt attention. “Your eyes are black again.” Maya circled him slowly. “And you’re not tracking my movement.” “I can see you from the knife.” Victor opened his physical eyes, and the dual perspective returned, overwhelming enough that he had to release the shadow vision to avoid nausea. They experimented for the next thirty minutes. Victor discovered he could view through any shadow within about fifty feet. Maya’s Battle Sense proved invaluable for understanding the mechanics. She described how his emotional signature shifted when he used Shadow Sight, how the ability registered to her System-enhanced perception as a splitting of presence. “This is exactly what I needed.” Maya’s excitement was palpable. “Battle Sense is picking up complexity it couldn’t before. It’s making the ability work harder, pushing it to distinguish between different types of danger.” “Can you use Shadow Stalker while I track you?” Maya hefted her axe. “I need practice against enemies I can’t see normally. That should really stress Battle Sense.” Victor activated Shadow Stalker and felt the darkness respond. Maya’s eyes went slightly unfocused, her head tilting as Battle Sense took over from normal vision. “I can feel you there. The threat signature is strong. But I can’t pinpoint exactly WHERE you are. Just a general area.” Victor moved silently to her left. Maya tracked the movement, but her orientation was off by several feet. She swung toward empty air, frustration building. An idea formed as Victor watched her struggle. He focused on a shadow pooled near Maya’s feet and tried making it produce sound. His first attempts yielded nothing. Then he adjusted his approach, treating it like Dread Weaving but for vibration rather than solid form. A faint scraping sound emerged from the shadow. Barely audible, like fabric dragging across concrete, but present. Maya spun toward it immediately. “There! I felt that!” Her Battle Sense had locked onto the sound’s location with far more precision than it could track Victor’s invisible form. Victor experimented further, creating different sounds in different shadows. Footsteps. Breathing. The rustle of movement. Each one produced distinct reactions from Maya’s Battle Sense, helping her correlate what she sensed with actual positions. “You’re making sounds in the shadows.” Maya lowered her axe slightly, fascination overtaking combat readiness. “Battle Sense is picking up the difference between real threats and created sounds. It’s learning to distinguish. This is perfect.” They tested the new skill systematically. Victor could create ambient sounds within fifty feet, though volume was limited, and complex noises were beyond his current capability. The cost was sustainable at five Dread per minute of active sound creation. “Can you make it look like something?” Maya had moved to the parking lot’s edge, catching her breath. “Not just sounds, but visible? If Battle Sense has to track multiple opponents simultaneously, that should really push it toward ranking up.” Victor’s first attempt produced a vaguely humanoid blob of darkness that looked more like melted wax than a person. Maya laughed outright. “I’m supposed to be scared of that?” “It’s a work in progress.” Victor tried again, this time focusing on the feeling of presence that his Life Sense read from living beings. The second attempt was marginally better. Maya’s Battle Sense registered it as a potential threat, though she could tell immediately it wasn’t genuine. “That one felt almost real until I looked at it directly. Battle Sense picked it up as a separate presence from you.” Victor refined his approach over the next twenty minutes. Maya described what her Battle Sense read from him when he moved, the specific qualities of weight and intent that created his threat signature. He tried replicating those qualities in the shadow constructs, adding details gradually until the illusions improved. The cost was high. Ten Dread to create a basic figure, three per minute to maintain it. But when Victor finally produced a shadow illusion that made Maya’s Battle Sense react as if facing a real opponent, the satisfaction was worth the expenditure. He created three figures around her position and watched Maya engage them in combat. Her Battle Sense eventually distinguished the real Victor from the illusions, but the initial confusion was valuable training. She learned to read threat signatures more precisely, to prioritize actual dangers over phantom ones. Iron Skin activated when real strikes came close. Warrior’s Resolve kept her calm despite fighting multiple angles simultaneously. Victor found himself proud watching her adapt. She was already better than before tonight’s training, already understanding Battle Sense in ways that would keep her alive in real combat. They collapsed side by side on the parking lot’s edge as dawn approached. Victor’s Dread pool sat at forty out of one hundred forty, depleted but not dangerously low. Maya was bruised and exhausted, sweat soaking through her armor, but her expression carried satisfaction. “Thank you.” Maya’s voice was softer now, tired but genuine. “Battle Sense feels different. Sharper. Like tonight pushed it closer to something better.” “You adapted fast.” Victor created one final shadow construct, a simple blade he could view through. “The ability responded to pressure exactly like you thought it would.” “You helped me create that pressure.” Maya leaned back against the concrete, her shoulder brushing his. “And I think I helped you discover abilities you didn’t know you had.” The truth of that settled between them. Shadow Sight. Shadow Sound. Shadow Illusions. Except they weren’t three separate abilities at all, Victor realized. They were applications of something deeper. Shadow Manipulation. A fundamental aspect of what the Noxborne transformation had made him. The System hadn’t granted these as discrete skills. His race had granted him dominion over darkness itself, and he was only beginning to understand what that meant. He would never have discovered it alone, would never have thought to explore the limits without Maya pushing him. “Jennifer’s lucky to have you,” Maya said quietly, her exhaustion making the words come easier. Moonlight softened her features, and Victor found himself noticing details he’d been too focused to see before. Strands of black hair that had escaped her braid. A slight tremor in her hands from fatigue. How close she was sitting. Victor felt something tighten in his chest, an emotion he couldn’t quite name and wasn’t sure he should. “I’m the lucky one. You both keep me human.” His voice came out rougher than intended. “Tonight… watching you push yourself, trusting me to help you. That meant something.” Maya turned to look at him fully, and the space between them felt suddenly charged. “You see us, Victor. Really see us. Not as people to protect, but as…” She paused, searching for words. “As equals walking beside you.” “You make it easy,” Victor said quietly. “Both of you do.” Maya’s hand found his, light and tentative. Neither of them moved away. They sat like that as the moon hung in the sky, the contact saying what neither was quite ready to say aloud. Maya’s breathing was already slowing, the adrenaline finally draining away completely. Her head tilted sideways, finding his shoulder with the unconscious trust of someone too tired to maintain distance. Victor went still. The gesture was casual, born of exhaustion rather than intention, but it carried meaning anyway. Maya trusted him enough to be vulnerable like this, to let her guard down completely. The same trust Sarah had shown by calling him Monty. The same trust Jennifer demonstrated every time she followed him into danger. He sat motionless as Maya’s breathing deepened into sleep, watching sunrise paint Havenport’s ruins in shades of orange and gold. The city looked almost beautiful in the early light, the destruction softened by distance and dawn’s forgiving glow. The sun climbed higher. Maya stirred finally, lifting her head with a mumbled apology. Victor waved off. They returned to the apartment as morning fully broke, both moving with the careful stiffness of people who’d pushed their bodies hard. Jennifer was awake when they entered, sitting at the kitchen table with coffee already brewing. She looked between them with knowing eyes, taking in the exhaustion and satisfaction both carried. Her gaze lingered on how close they were standing, how Maya’s armor still bore scuff marks from training, how Victor’s eyes carried a warmth that hadn’t been there when they’d left. “Good training?” she asked, and something in her tone suggested she was asking about more than combat practice. “Very good.” Maya collapsed onto the couch with a satisfied groan, color rising in her cheeks under Jennifer’s scrutiny. “Victor taught me some new tricks. And I think Battle Sense might be close to ranking up.” Victor moved to the table, pouring himself coffee from the French press. His fingers brushed Jennifer’s as he reached for a mug, the contact lingering a fraction longer than necessary. “She taught me some too.” Jennifer’s smile turned knowing. She stood and moved closer to Victor, her hand finding his arm with casual intimacy. “So when do I get my date with you? Or does Maya get all the quality time?” “It wasn’t a date.” Maya’s protest came too fast, her voice pitching slightly higher. She sat up straighter on the couch, fingers fidgeting with her armor straps. “It was training. Combat practice. Very serious skill development.” “Mm-hmm.” Jennifer’s eyes sparkled with amusement. She glanced back at Maya, then up at Victor, her expression soft despite the teasing. “All night. Under the stars. Just the two of you discovering new abilities together. Very serious.” Victor felt heat creep up his neck. Maya had gone pink, determinedly studying her boots with sudden intensity. The apartment felt smaller somehow, the air charged with something unspoken. “I want a training date too.” Jennifer’s voice dropped lower, more serious despite the playful words. Her hand slid down Victor’s arm to lace her fingers through his. “Just you and me. Pushing limits. Finding out what we can do together.” The weight of her gaze made Victor’s breath catch. Maya had gone very still on the couch, watching them both with an expression caught between longing and uncertainty. The moment stretched, heavy with possibility. Victor looked between them. Jennifer’s hand warm in his, her eyes holding promises of things neither had been brave enough to name. Maya on the couch, fierce and beautiful and waiting to see what he’d say. Both of them were his in ways that went beyond simple protection or friendship. “Tomorrow night,” Victor said quietly, meeting Jennifer’s eyes. “Just us. I’ll show you everything I learned.” Jennifer’s smile could have lit the darkened city. Her free hand rose to touch his face, thumb brushing along his jaw in a gesture that felt dangerously intimate. “I’m holding you to that.” Maya cleared her throat softly from the couch. When Victor looked at her, she was smiling despite the pink still coloring her cheeks. “Next time we train, you’re teaching me those shadow illusions properly. All of them.” “Deal,” he rasped, and the sound carried an unmistakable hunger he hadn’t meant to reveal. The three of them stayed like that as morning light filled the apartment. Jennifer’s hand in his, her body warm against his side. Maya watching from the couch with eyes that said she understood exactly what was growing between the three of them. The world outside remained dangerous and uncertain. But in this moment, in this space they’d carved from chaos, something new was taking shape. Something that felt like home.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Chapter 34- The Runner
  https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/149931/noxborne/chapter/3203948/chapter-34--the-runner
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Sarah’s latest drawing showed Victor with horns and wings, breathing fire at what appeared to be a very upset goblin. The artistic liberty made Jennifer laugh outright when Sarah held it up with both hands, her smile gap-toothed and proud. Victor studied the image with the same serious attention he’d given every piece of artwork Sarah had produced in the three days since they’d left her at Fort Resistance. “The wings are a nice touch.” He crouched to her level, the movement smooth despite the Shroud Coat’s weight. “Though I don’t think I can actually fly.” “Maybe you can if you try really hard.” Sarah’s blue eyes held the absolute certainty of childhood logic. “Monsters can do lots of things.” “That’s true.” Victor accepted the drawing with careful hands, folding it precisely so the crayon wouldn’t smudge. “I’ll keep this one safe.” Sarah beamed and immediately dove back into her collection of salvaged art supplies, already planning her next masterpiece. The Graces watched from their apartment’s doorway with the patient fondness of people who’d discovered that caring for a traumatized six-year-old was both exhausting and rewarding in equal measure. Julia Grace caught Victor’s eye and mouthed a silent thank you that he acknowledged with a slight nod. Fort Resistance hummed with morning activity beyond the residential section. Voices carried through the converted clinic’s halls as survivors organized supply runs and guard rotations. The settlement had grown to nearly forty people since Victor’s first visit, Sandra’s reputation for fair dealing and actual medical care drawing refugees from across Havenport’s residential zones. The walls showed fresh reinforcement where James and other scouts had identified weak points. Gardens sprouted in every available patch of dirt, careful cultivation replacing the chaos that had consumed the city. Sandra appeared in the hallway with two steaming mugs, her movements carrying the practiced efficiency of someone who’d spent fifty years navigating hospital corridors. The Reginite evolution had frozen her physical age somewhere in the early thirties, but the way she handed Victor coffee spoke to decades of reading people. Her eyes tracked across his features with clinical assessment. “You look better.” She offered the second mug to Jennifer. “Last time you were here, you had that hollow look. Underfed.” Victor accepted the observation without commenting. His reservoir sat at 42 out of 50, the feeding on Market enforcers and opportunistic goblin kills maintaining sustainable levels. The hunger remained present but manageable, a background awareness rather than urgent demand. His skin had better color. The slight gauntness that had marked his cheeks during early transformation had filled out. He looked healthy in ways that four days ago would have seemed impossible. “The city provides.” Maya’s voice carried dark humor as she joined them from the clinic’s main room. “Plenty of things that deserve killing.” Sandra’s expression suggested she understood exactly what that meant but chose not to pursue the topic. She led them toward her office, the small room dominated by maps covering every wall. Havenport’s streets were marked with colored pins and annotations that tracked faction territories, safe routes, and threat concentrations. Red pins clustered north of the river where Thrag’s hobgoblins had established their boundary. Blue marked Cross’s military operation at Central Station. Green showed Fort Resistance and allied survivor groups. Black pins scattered throughout indicated known danger zones. “Supply situation is stable.” Sandra traced routes on the nearest map. “We’ve established trade with three other survivor groups. Medical services in exchange for food, building materials, defense coordination.” Her finger stopped at Central Station. “General Cross’s people have been professional. They patrol the areas between their position and ours, keep the hobgoblin scouts from pushing too far south.” “You’ve met her?” Jennifer leaned closer to study the map. “Twice. She’s competent, disciplined, genuinely trying to save people.” Sandra’s tone carried respect mixed with reservation. “But she sees everything through a military lens. Order, chain of command, following proper procedure even when the world’s ended.” Her eyes found Victor. “You two wouldn’t get along.” Victor had heard enough about General Cross from various sources to form a picture. Former military officer, Level 7 Sentinel, running a sixty-person operation with rigid hierarchy and defined rules of engagement. She’d apparently been asking questions about the Dark Elf, the mysterious figure who killed Market slavers and left dried corpses in parking garages. Sandra hadn’t confirmed Victor’s identity to Cross’s people, but the doctor was too perceptive to miss the connection. “As long as she stays in her territory and I stay in mine, we don’t need to get along.” Victor studied the map, noting how Central Station’s position controlled transit routes through Havenport’s central districts. Strategic location. Losing it would mean hobgoblin forces could push anywhere without organized resistance. James appeared in the doorway, his recovered shoulder showing no sign of the slaver’s blade that had nearly killed him four days ago. The scout had proven invaluable to Fort Resistance, his knowledge of Havenport’s layout and willingness to range far from safety providing early warning of threats. His expression now carried tension that made Victor’s Life Sense sharpen automatically. “Increased hobgoblin activity north of the river.” James moved to the map, pointing at several locations. “Not Thrag’s usual patrols. Different armor, different markings. I counted maybe forty in concentrated groups, all moving with purpose.” His finger traced a path. “They’re positioning around Central Station. Setting up siege lines.” The implications settled heavy in Sandra’s office. Victor’s mind catalogued the tactical reality. Sixty defenders at Central Station against organized hobgoblin forces with numerical superiority. Cross’s people were trained and disciplined, but levels and numbers would eventually overwhelm training. If Central Station fell, the hobgoblins would control transit routes and have clear paths to push into residential zones. Toward Fort Resistance. Toward Sarah. The sound came before anyone could respond to James’s report. Footsteps, stumbling and desperate, echoing through Fort Resistance’s main corridor. Victor’s Life Sense caught the emotional signature before the person appeared. Terror mixed with pain, determination fighting exhaustion, the complex fear of someone who’d survived something horrific and kept moving anyway. The runner collapsed through Sandra’s doorway more than entered. Male, early twenties, wearing battered armor that might have been military surplus before Integration. Blood soaked his left side from a wound that looked like claws had opened his ribs. His face was pale with blood loss, eyes wide with the kind of fear that came from seeing friends die. Sandra moved immediately, hands already glowing golden as she caught him before he hit the floor completely. “Get him to the medical bay. Now.” Victor and James supported the runner between them, carrying him through Fort Resistance’s halls while Sandra ran ahead to prepare. Survivors stopped their morning activities to watch, recognizing the armor, understanding what a wounded soldier from Central Station meant. The runner’s breathing came in ragged gasps, each inhale obviously painful. Victor’s Life Sense read the injury’s severity. Without Sandra’s healing, the man had maybe an hour before blood loss or infection killed him. This story originates from a different website. Ensure the author gets the support they deserve by reading it there. They laid him on the medical bed and Sandra’s regeneration field washed over the wounds immediately. Golden light seeped into torn flesh, knitting muscle and sealing blood vessels. The runner’s gasping eased as pain diminished, consciousness returning as his body stabilized. His eyes found Sandra’s face with desperate intensity. “Central Station. Under siege.” The words came between controlled breaths as healing progressed. “Hundreds of them. Hobgoblins. Not like the scouts we’ve been dealing with. These are organized. Coordinated. Led by something massive.” Jennifer and Maya had followed from Sandra’s office, both listening with focused attention. Victor felt his reservoir pulse with awareness, the hunger stirring slightly as he processed the threat level. Hundreds of hobgoblins. Led by something massive. The description matched nothing they’d encountered before. “General Cross?” Sandra’s hands stayed steady as she worked, but her voice carried urgency. “Holding the main concourse. We’ve got maybe forty effective fighters left.” The runner’s eyes squeezed shut with memory. “Lost twenty in the first assault. This thing leading them, it’s huge. Seven feet, maybe more. Bone armor. Axe that could split a car.” His breathing hitched despite the healing. “It challenged Cross directly. Called her out. She’s trying to hold the line but we’re outnumbered four to one.” The golden light continued its work, closing the worst of the claw wounds. Sandra’s face showed the concentration required to heal injuries this severe, decades of medical expertise guiding System-granted regeneration. Victor watched the runner’s emotional signature shift from desperate terror toward exhausted relief. The man would survive. Whether Central Station would survive was a different question. “Cross sent me for reinforcements.” The runner’s voice strengthened as his wounds sealed. “Any help you can provide. We can’t hold another day without support.” Sandra finished the healing and stepped back, her expression troubled. She looked at Victor with an intensity that made clear she wasn’t asking as a doctor, but as someone responsible for forty lives that depended on Fort Resistance’s safety. “I know you and Cross won’t see eye to eye. Different philosophies, different methods. But if Central Station falls, the hobgoblins will push south. This settlement is directly in their path.” Victor’s Life Sense swept the room automatically, reading emotional signatures. Jennifer’s concern mixed with determination. Maya’s readiness for combat overlaid with strategic assessment. Sandra’s worry for her people. The runner’s fading terror giving way to hope that someone might actually help. And beyond the medical bay, throughout Fort Resistance, the ambient fear of forty survivors who’d built something fragile and precious that could be destroyed in hours if the wrong forces moved south. His mind calculated distances and threat levels with cold precision. Central Station was twelve blocks north. If it fell, hobgoblins would have clear routes through Havenport’s center. Fort Resistance had walls and defenders, but not enough to withstand a sustained assault by hundreds of organized enemies. Sandra’s healing abilities were invaluable but wouldn’t matter if they were overwhelmed by sheer numbers. Sarah was here. Playing with crayons in the Graces’ apartment, drawing pictures of her monster who kept the bad things away. Trusting completely that Monty would protect her from whatever dangers threatened. “This isn’t our fight.” The words came out harder than Victor intended. “Cross runs a military operation. She has trained soldiers, defensive positions, resources. We’re three people.” “Three very capable people.” Sandra’s voice stayed level. “Who’ve been clearing threats across residential zones for days. The Dark Elf’s reputation reaches even Cross’s operation.” Victor met her eyes, seeing the calculation there. Sandra knew exactly who he was, what he’d done to Market slavers and goblin nests. She was leveraging that knowledge now, asking him to become the thing he’d been avoiding. A hero. Someone who fought for strangers because it was right, not just because it protected his immediate circle. “The monsters are coming here, aren’t they?” The small voice carried from the medical bay’s entrance. Sarah stood with Julia Grace, her blue eyes wide with the fear children felt when the adults couldn’t hide their worry anymore. “To Fort Resistance?” Victor’s chest tightened. Sarah shouldn’t be here, shouldn’t be hearing this. But Julia’s apologetic expression made clear the girl had slipped away while the Graces were distracted by the runner’s arrival. Sarah walked forward with careful steps, her stuffed rabbit clutched tight, looking up at Victor with absolute trust that felt like physical weight. “But Monty will protect us, right?” Her voice carried the certainty of someone who’d never been let down by a promise. “You keep the bad things away.” The trust in those words hit harder than any argument Sandra could make. Victor had promised to be Sarah’s monster, the kind that kept other monsters at bay. That promise extended to Fort Resistance whether he’d articulated it or not. If hobgoblins pushed south and Sarah died because he’d refused to address a threat while it was still manageable, the promise meant nothing. Jennifer’s hand found his arm, the touch grounding. Maya shifted her weight with the subtle movement of someone ready to follow his decision regardless of what it was. Both of them watching, waiting, trusting him to choose correctly even when the right choice wasn’t obvious. Victor crouched to Sarah’s level, meeting her eyes directly. “The monsters won’t reach Fort Resistance. I promise.” Sarah’s smile was bright and immediate, the fear vanishing like it had never existed. She hugged him quickly, then ran back to Julia Grace, already chattering about the new drawing she wanted to make. Victor stood slowly, his decision made even as doubt coiled in his stomach. “We’ll scout Central Station first.” He looked at Sandra. “Assess the actual threat, determine if intervention is viable. If it’s a suicide mission, we pull back and focus on evacuating Fort Resistance before the hobgoblins push south.” Sandra nodded, relief visible in her expression. “That’s all I’m asking. Make an informed decision.” The runner sat up on the medical bed, color returning to his face. “General Cross needs to know help is coming.” “Don’t tell her who we are.” Victor’s tone left no room for argument. “Tell her reinforcements are scouting the situation. Nothing more.” The preparations took fifteen minutes. Victor checked his Shadow Fangs, confirming the blades moved smoothly in their sheaths. His Shroud Coat showed minor damage from previous fights but remained functional, the System-granted armor already slowly repairing itself in darkness. Jennifer verified her mana pool sat at full capacity after a night’s rest. Maya’s fire axe hung ready at her belt, the runes along its blade dormant but prepared to channel flames on command. Sandra provided what intelligence she had from previous runner reports. Central Station’s layout, Cross’s defensive positions, the approximate number of hobgoblin forces observed. It wasn’t much, but Victor committed the details to memory anyway. James offered again to accompany them, and again Victor refused. Fort Resistance needed experienced defenders if things went wrong. Sarah intercepted them at the gate, Mr. Hopps tucked under one arm. She thrust a folded piece of paper at Victor with both hands. “For luck. So you remember to come back.” Victor unfolded the drawing. Sarah had worked fast. The image showed three figures standing together, recognizable as Victor, Jennifer, and Maya despite the crayon medium’s limitations. Above them in large letters, she’d written COME BACK SAFE. “I will.” Victor folded the drawing carefully and tucked it inside his Shroud Coat. “I promise.” The gate closed behind them as they headed north. Fort Resistance’s walls faded into the transformed cityscape, replaced by Havenport’s new reality. Abandoned cars serving as goblin shelters. Buildings marked with territorial scratches. The occasional survivor watching from upper windows, too afraid to come down. Victor’s Life Sense swept ahead constantly, painting emotional maps through solid walls. Fear everywhere, anxiety radiating from boarded windows. But also something else in the distance. Concentrated aggression. Organized malice. The emotional signatures of hundreds of creatures unified in purpose, all focused on a single location. Central Station. The siege James had reported was real and ongoing. Victor could feel it from blocks away, the sheer weight of hostile intent pressing against defensive positions. Cross’s forces were holding, but barely. The emotional signatures from human defenders carried exhaustion mixing with determination, people fighting because retreat meant death for everyone behind them. “So we’re helping Cross?” Jennifer’s question came after three blocks of silence. Victor activated Shadow Stalker, darkness pooling around all three of them as they moved through morning streets. “We’re protecting Fort Resistance. If that means helping Cross survive, then that’s what we do.” Maya checked her weapons with practiced efficiency. “Think she’ll be grateful?” Victor’s smile held no warmth, just dark amusement at the question. “I doubt it. But she’ll owe us.” His Life Sense tracked the concentrated threat ahead, measuring distances and calculating odds. “And that might matter before this is over.” They continued north toward Central Station, three people moving through shadows toward a siege that could determine Havenport’s future. Above them, morning sun climbed higher over the transformed city. Somewhere ahead, General Cross held a defensive line against overwhelming numbers. Somewhere in that chaos, something massive waited. A warchief leading hundreds of hobgoblins with the tactical intelligence to actually threaten organized human resistance. Victor’s reservoir pulsed with awareness. The hunger stirred, recognizing that complex intelligent fear waited ahead. Enemies worth feeding on. Threats that needed eliminating. His hands found his Shadow Fangs without conscious thought, the blades familiar and ready. Sarah’s drawing pressed against his chest through the Shroud Coat’s inner pocket. A promise made. A responsibility accepted. The weight of trust from a six-year-old who called him Monty and believed completely that monsters could be good if they protected the right people. Victor intended to prove her right.
