Chapter 67 Amazed by a Modern Future She Couldn’t Imagine Alex and Astrid pressed onward toward High Hrothgar, their path winding ever upward in the direction of the Throat of the World. The road beneath their boots was long and uneven, carved through ancient stone and frozen soil. With every step they took together, the distance between their hearts seemed to shrink. Their shoulders occasionally brushed, their pace unconsciously syncing, as if neither of them wanted to walk ahead of the other. They were already far along the journey now, nearly within sight of Whiterun. At first, delicate snowflakes drifted lazily from the sky, catching in Astrid’s hair and melting against Alex’s cloak. The air was crisp and sharp, carrying the scent of pine and cold stone. But as they continued forward and the terrain gradually shifted mountains giving way to open plains the snowfall thinned, then faded entirely. The sky lightened, and the world around them felt calmer, quieter, almost welcoming. Along the way, Alex remained focused on one thing: grinding his Alteration skill. Without breaking his stride, he repeatedly cast Telekinesis, lifting pebbles, loose chunks of ice, and stray branches into the air beside him. Objects floated, spun slowly, then dropped as his mana ran dry. Each time, he exhaled, rolled his shoulders, and waited patiently for the familiar warmth of mana to flow back into his body. The moment it recovered, he did it again. Invisible force wrapped around another object, dragging it into the air as if held by an unseen hand. Alex’s expression was calm but intent, his eyes slightly narrowed in concentration, fingers subtly flexing as he maintained control while walking. Alteration 32 → 33. He stopped for a brief moment, planting his feet firmly on the path. His breath fogged in the cold air as he waited, feeling the last traces of mana drain away. A few heartbeats later, energy returned and once more, objects rose and trembled in the air around him. Again and again, the cycle repeated. Alteration 33 → 35 → 37 → 40 → 42. With each increase, the spell felt smoother, more responsive, as if the magic itself was becoming an extension of his body. Stones floated higher, steadier. The strain on his mind lessened. Even Astrid could feel the subtle shift in the air around him the pressure, the hum of controlled power growing stronger with every step they took toward the mountain. Astrid slowed her steps slightly, her gaze lingering on Alex instead of the road ahead. Her brows knit together as she watched stone after stone rise into the air beside him, hovering obediently before dropping back down. The faint shimmer of magic around his hand never seemed to fade, and the spell didn’t falter even as he walked. She tilted her head, arms crossing over her chest, curiosity clearly written on her face. After a few more moments of silent observation, she finally spoke. “Ah…” she said slowly, eyes flicking from the floating object to Alex’s focused expression. “So this is why you’re so obsessed with spells and magic, huh?” She raised an eyebrow at him. “Because in your world, none of this exists, right?” Alex turned his head slightly toward her, though his hand remained outstretched. The object he was holding with Telekinesis trembled for a moment, then steadied as he tightened his control. A faint smirk tugged at the corner of his lips. “Yep. Exactly,” he replied casually. “If people from my world were here, they’d probably do the same thing I’m doing right now.” Astrid’s eyes widened just a little. She slowed even more, boots crunching softly against the gravel as she imagined it. One hand rose to her chin, her fingers tapping thoughtfully. “Hmmm…” she hummed. “A world without magic sounds… kind of difficult.” Alex let out a soft breath through his nose, still watching the object float and spin. His eyes never fully left the spell. “Not really,” he said. “Our world is already modern. We’ve developed a lot of advanced tools.” He flicked his wrist slightly, sending the object drifting forward before letting it fall. Then he glanced at her again. “For example can you communicate over long distances in this world?” he asked. “Let’s say you’re in Whiterun and I’m in Winterhold. Can we talk to each other?” Astrid stopped walking altogether. Her eyes drifted upward as she searched her mind for an answer, lips pursed in concentration. “Hmmm…” she murmured. “Smoke signals, maybe?” Her eyes suddenly lit up. “Oh! Oh! What about something like the Greybeards!?” She paused mid-thought, her expression twisting as realization hit. “Eh… but that still uses magic,” she muttered, shoulders slumping slightly. “Hmm… how would that even work?” Alex couldn’t help but smile. He shook his head lightly, amusement clear on his face. “If we used the Greybeards’ method,” he said, “the sky would be unbearably noisy. And besides, they only shout out a single word.” Astrid groaned softly, rubbing her temples as if trying to force the answer out of her own head. She stared at the ground, then at Alex, then back at the sky thinking, thinking, thinking. Finally, she let out a long sigh and raised both hands in surrender. “Okay,” she said, shaking her head with a small laugh. “I can’t imagine it. I give up.” She looked at Alex directly now, eyes bright with curiosity and challenge. “So… how do you do it?” Alex let out a low, amused chuckle, rubbing the back of his neck as his shoulders relaxed. “Hehehe… honestly,” he admitted, his tone light and a little sheepish, “I don’t really know how to make it either.” Astrid stopped in her tracks. Her eyes narrowed, a sharp glare locking onto Alex. The air around her seemed to tense as she lifted her hand, fingers curling into a half-fist, clearly preparing to smack the top of his head. “Idiot!! ” Alex reacted instantly. The Telekinesis spell vanished mid-cast, the floating object dropping harmlessly to the ground. He ducked slightly and threw both arms up, covering his face and head defensively. “H-hey, hey! Wait!” he blurted out. “I had the tool back then, okay!? Don’t hit me yet at least let me explain first!” Astrid clicked her tongue, her raised hand trembling with restraint before she finally lowered it. She crossed her arms tightly over her chest, tapping her foot against the ground in irritation. If you spot this narrative on Amazon, know that it has been stolen. Report the violation. “Then how are you supposed to explain it,” she snapped, “if you don’t even know how it’s made?” Alex straightened up, letting out a slow breath as he regained his composure. His expression shifted less playful, more thoughtful. “I don’t know how to build it,” he said calmly, meeting her eyes, “but I understand the concept behind it.” He cleared his throat, rolled his shoulders back, and lifted a finger as if beginning a lecture. His tone became more deliberate, more serious. “Astrid,” he said, “do you know what waves or vibrations are?” Astrid paused. Instead of answering with words, she smirked slightly and turned her body sideways. With a sharp breath and a sudden thrust of her hand, she released Unrelenting Force. The air exploded outward. A nearby tree shook violently, its branches whipping back and forth as leaves scattered across the ground. Alex watched the display without surprise, nodding in approval once the wind settled. “Yeah,” he said, impressed. “It’s kind of like that.” He gestured toward his mouth as he continued. “But in my world, we can’t produce waves that powerful with our mouths. Instead, we manipulate the sound waves that come out when we speak.” Astrid frowned slightly, her head tilting as confusion crept into her expression. Seeing this, Alex took a step closer and continued gently. “You’ve been in a cave before, right?” he asked. “When you shout, your voice echoes back to you.” Her eyes widened, and she snapped her fingers as realization struck. “Ah right!” she said. “In caves, your voice repeats over and over.” “Exactly,” Alex replied, nodding. “That’s sound waves bouncing back. Every word we speak travels through the air as waves.” He lowered his hand and looked ahead at the road, his voice carrying a hint of nostalgia as he went on. “Long ago, information in our world became incredibly important. News had to be delivered quickly, no matter the distance. Because of that need, people found a way to solve the problem.” He turned back to Astrid with a small, confident smile. “And that solution,” he said, “was something we called a telephone.” Astrid’s eyes sparkled with pure amazement, the earlier irritation completely gone. She leaned forward slightly, hands clasped in front of her chest as if she were afraid to miss even a single word. “Wow…” she breathed, her voice soft with wonder. “What does a telephone look like?” Alex answered casually, but there was a hint of pride in his tone. He lifted a hand, gesturing as if holding an invisible object. “It changed a lot as technology advanced,” he explained. “But in my era, telephones were small small enough to fit in one hand and easy to carry anywhere.” He mimed slipping something into his pocket, then continued. “Not only could we talk to people over long distances, but we didn’t even need to bring books anymore. Everything knowledge, maps, stories was stored inside the phone itself. In the modern age, we called it a smartphone.” Astrid slowly looked up at the sky, her eyes reflecting the drifting clouds above. A faint smile formed on her lips, as if she were trying to picture that distant world. “That sounds incredible…” she murmured dreamily. “I’m really curious about your world. I wonder… could I ever see a phone or a smartphone here?” Alex watched her quietly for a moment. There was a trace of sympathy in his gaze regret, perhaps, that he couldn’t simply hand her one. Then his eyes suddenly brightened. “Ah,” he said, snapping his fingers. “I’ve got an idea.” He turned to her with a playful grin. “Want me to show you how a telephone works?” Astrid’s head snapped back toward him instantly. Her eyes lit up, shining with excitement, and she leaned closer. “Yes! Yes!” she said eagerly, nodding so fast her hair swayed. “I want to!” Alex laughed, the sound light and warm. “Hehe, alright. Let me take this out first.” He reached into his inventory, and with a faint shimmer, pulled out two thin metal cups and a long piece of string. He held them up like a magician revealing props. “Okay,” he said, placing them into Astrid’s hands. “We need these materials. Can you help me make a small hole in the bottom of each cup? The hole has to fit the string perfectly.” Astrid’s lips curved into a confident smile. She drew her dagger, positioning the cup carefully, then gave it a precise, controlled strike. Clink. The metal pierced cleanly. She handed it back proudly. Alex nodded in approval. “Yes, that’s perfect.” He threaded the string through both cups, fingers moving deftly, then tied the ends securely. He lifted the finished creation with a flourish. “Tada it’s done.” Astrid stared at it in silence. Her excitement slowly faded into a flat, unimpressed look. Her shoulders slumped. “…Huh?” she said. “What’s so amazing about this?” Alex grinned brightly, clearly enjoying her disappointment. His eyes gleamed with mischief. “Alright,” he said, chuckling. “Now step far away from me and pull the string tight. Make it completely straight.” Still doubtful, Astrid did as he said, walking backward until the string stretched fully between them, taut and straight. Once she stopped, Alex raised his voice. “See!?” he shouted. “I need to shout for my voice to reach you like this!” Astrid cupped one hand around her mouth and shouted back, her voice echoing across the open road. “Yeah!!” She still didn’t quite get it but curiosity had already taken hold, and she leaned closer to the strange little device, waiting for what he would do next. Alex raised his voice again, one hand cupped around his mouth while the other kept the string taut. “Now put your ear against the cup!” Astrid hesitated for half a heartbeat, then obeyed. She lifted the cool metal cup and pressed it gently to her ear, her brows knitting in concentration. At the same moment, Alex brought the other cup close to his lips. His posture relaxed, his voice dropping from a shout to something soft and intimate. “Astrid… I love you.” The words traveled through the string. Astrid froze as if struck by lightning.   18. The Salvager's Plague
  Tác giả: TheAcornScribe
  Tổng chương: 68
  Ngày cập nhật: N/A
  URL: https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/101168/the-salvagers-plague
▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓

  Mô tả: Salvage Engineer Thomas Aacen swore he'd never work with Captain Barstol again, but was blackmailed into it. The blackmail was falsified, but even still, it might have been better to just let him spill it, cause now he was abandoned for dead on a remote planetoid with no atmosphere, desperately looking for a way to survive. But that is just the beginning of his troubles, when his attempt to survive unearths the truth about the ship Captain Barstol just hauled away. A truth that would put the whole quadrant in danger.

There was a brief kerfuffle when we got to the massive doors that led into the area of the outpost dedicated to the top secret research surrounding the alien - re: giobhioni - site. When Controy tried to use his access card to allow us entry, big red holographic letters exclaiming “Access Denied” across the entire surface of the door. When he tried again, a klaxon went off, likely intended to summon security personnel from behind the door. “This is getting extremely tiresome, Captain.” I yelled over the alarm, eyeing the lock interface and wondering how long it would take me to cobble together something to allow Stacy access to it. “I agree wholeheartedly!” he responded, looking just a bit pissed off himself. “Admiral LeBeau sent complete authorization to get me into this section!” A moment later, the klaxon cut off, and a voice came through the door’s address system. “Captain Controy, you are not cleared to access the labs. I will be logging this attempt with the Colonel.” “I’ve had just about enough of this.” He snarled. “Give me your name, soldier, so I can report it to the JAG officers when they arrive. The Admiral already plans to see anyone in this facility who has sworn allegiance to wanted terrorists spaced or hung.” There was silence for a long moment, then “The Admiral does not have the authority to alter the command structure here. It must come directly from…” “The Prime Minister. Yes, so you folk keep saying.” Controy snapped, sounding on the verge of going nuclear. “The orders were given with the prime minister’s authorization codes. Now open this door before I get a full marine squad down here with breeching charges and blow the whole damned thing to atoms.” “Sorry Captain, but it has to come directly from…” Jophixa stepped up beside the captain, “Captain, allow me.” she turned to the door, her hand casually flipping her kaon lance once before pointing it at the door. “I am Commander Jophixa Throhx of the Giobhioni republic. You are illegally keeping artifacts of my people, possibly desecrating them with your experiments. If you do not open this door within the next three seconds, you had best run like you are trying to escape the Void’s very maw, because I have to open it, you’re going to get caught in the backwash.” “Fuck off, Xeno.” the person on the other side of the door snapped, “This is a blast door. Good luck breaching it.” “Well, that settles it.” I sighed. “Another of Fisch’s humanis scumbags. The fleet needs a caustic enema.” Jo glanced at me and gave me one of her sharklike grins. “I can take care of this one for her at least.” then looked at the Captain as she quickly slipped her helmet on and sealed it. “I recommend we all take cover, and I apologize for the damage this is going to likely cause.” He was about to say something, possibly a comment about how the little cylinder she was holding couldn’t possibly do much damage. But when he saw the twins and I hurriedly ducking around the corner for cover - and donning our own helmets, just in case - he closed his mouth and joined us, pulling out earplugs from a pouch on his belt and slipping them into his ears. But just as I started to explain, Jo, who had followed the captain around the corner, leaned just far enough around it, and depressed the trigger on the lance. The sound was indescribable. So far, our use of the kaon lance had been on targets in the void of space, so there had been no way for us to hear any sounds created when the kaons struck. But here, in the atmosphere of the outpost, the sound, and accompanying shockwave, made me wonder if I was feeling what it would be like at ground zero of a fusion reactor going critical. When I looked over at Controy, his eyes were wide as fuel scoop ducts as he stared at Jo. “What in the intergalactic void is that thing?” he yelled. I glanced at Jo, who’s grin was so wide it made it look like her face was nothing but sharp, triangular teeth. The overwhelming look of joy on her face caused a grin to form on my own face, I wanted to grab her and kiss her soundly, but restrained myself. Instead I turned back to the captain and began, “That’s…” Jo and the twins both chimed in chorus, “Classified.” He just stared at us for a moment, pulled the earplugs out of his ears and peeked around the corner at the damage. “Sweet Singularities…” he whispered, and I glanced around the corner to survey the damage myself. I’d already seen the destruction the kaon lance could inflict on objects in space. The debris Jo had tested her handheld lance on, and seeing the ship mounted version on Void’s Cutlass cutting right through the reactor casing of the Journeyman and sending it blazing like a supernova - both instances were seen at a distance. This, however, was only a dozen or so meters away. Where the door had been, was now a gaping hole. I don’t mean that the door had a hole blasted through it, I mean that there was no door left. Hell, there were parts of the wall surrounding where the door had been that were missing. I could see the bare bedrock of Parthax beyond where the wall segments should have been, fragments of it still crumbling down onto the…I couldn’t even call it the floor anymore because that had been blasted away as well. There was simply a sphere of absolute ruin around where the door had been. Gertrude let out a long, soft whistle. “Damn,” she said quietly, stepped over and elbowed me in the side. “Gotta hand it to you Spannerman, that is one impressive bit of kit. I don’t suppose…” “You’ve already got one mounted to the Compass ‘Trude.” her sister interrupted, “Somehow I doubt you’ll be able to convince our Mistress of Destruction over there to let you convince him even if you were willing.” Gertrude looked over at Jo, who returned the look, still grinning like a mad hatter. Gertrude raised a questioning eyebrow, to which Jo tilted her head in thought for a moment, her ears held high and twitching slightly. After a few seconds, she gave a one shouldered shrug as if to say ‘we’ll talk’. I opened my mouth to say something, but Stacy cut in through my implant. Just go with the flow honeysuckle. You can deal with tall, blonde and stacked over there later. Yeah. We still had to figure out why the giobhioni were here, probably fight more of Fisch-face’s bought off traitors, and maybe, just maybe, find out what really happened to my Mom. It’s not like I had the time right this moment to build another kaon lance. Nor was it likely that anyone was going to be doing anything amorous until we got back to the ship. “Alright, if you guys are finished with…whatever that was, shouldn’t we get moving?” I looked at the Captain, “I’m going to warn you right now, the moment I get my hands on an interface to your research database here, I’m cracking that thing wide open and uploading the entire contents to Elegance. And I’m fairly certain if anyone tries to stop me…” Jo coughed, and tapped the kaon lance against her leg. The captain looked back and forth between us, then just shook his head. “Shall we get going?” he asked finally, “that racket you made just now is likely to have more of the security from that section come running.” Sure enough, we didn’t even make it past the remains of the door before a trio of soldiers appeared, coilgun rifles at the ready, and glaring at us through the wreckage. But the captain just glared back and said calmly, “Are we going to have a problem here gentlemen?” and motioned to the mess. They glanced around them, blinked, then looked back at Controy. “Nosir!” they said in unison, and put up their rifles. “We were told there was an attempted unauthorized entry.” The genuine version of this novel can be found on another site. Support the author by reading it there. “Indeed.” Controy replied, his voice hard, “A mutinous soldier decided the payments he received from a wanted extremist superseded the chain of command.” Again, he looked at the mess around him, “They won’t have the opportunity to learn from that mistake. Best not to repeat their errors.” “Sir, yessir!” “And just to prevent any foolish thoughts,” He continued, “see the man standing behind me? He’s got eyes in the back of his head. Try sneaking up behind us, and they might not even find any DNA to identify your remains. Report to the transportation platform, and take up positions there. Olsen’s team are in the brig pending court martial.” “Sir, yessir!” The trio took off at a trot, giving us only a slight glance as they passed by us. “I wonder if that was the group that was supposed to ambush us.” Giselle chuckled. A quiet laugh escaped from Jo’s mouth. “If they were, I hope the object lesson changed their minds about their loyalties.” When we headed down the corridor again, we all seemed to be anxious about what we were going to encounter. There was a general mood of wanting to hustle and get to the actual labs as soon as possible, but by unspoken agreement, we forced a careful pace. None of us wanted to be caught flatfooted if there was still an ambush waiting for us. Thankfully, it turned out that the ambush seemed to have been averted, and it was only a few dozen meters down the corridor when we entered into some sort of observation room. It was mostly a plain white room, well lit, with a long conference table in the center of it. A holomonitor projected a set of data points and graphs, lines of information that vaguely reminded me of my station in engineering back on Elegance, displaying readouts from the various systems. I didn’t recognize any of the labels, however. The far wall, across the conference table from us, was almost entirely transparent, and even with the table in the way, I could tell that it overlooked another room. A sense of dread started to claw its way up my spine. Stacy… Is Tratsa finished with the implants? She just wrapped up. Everything went fine, and will come out of anesthesia in an hour or so. You might want to ask her to get to a monitor and run my feeds to her. I said, slowly making my way around the conference table, I have a really bad feeling… Right, she’s still in Med-bay. I’m routing the feeds now. I glanced over at Jo, to see that she once again had her hand on the kaon lance, following me around the conference table with the same trepidation showing in every milligram of her body language. “Tsaki Tratsa is monitoring my sensor feeds.” I told her, glanced at the captain, then stepped the rest of the way to the ‘window’. Below was some sort of operating theater. It wasn’t quite as high tech as the one on board Elegance, but it was far above any I’d seen outside futuristic holocasts, with the walls covered in holomonitors, and two highly articulated robot arms aiding the five staff that were bent over an operating table. Between each of them were carts containing both surgical tools, as well as what looked like some sort of small stasis units. At first, I couldn’t see past the surgical staff - if that’s what they were - to see what they were working on, and I adjusted my position a couple times to get a better look. It was just so frustrating, they almost seemed to be deliberately blocking their view of their subject. But just as I was getting ready to scream at them to move, one of them stepped back and placed something that looked disturbingly like a lung into one of the miniature stasis units. “What the FUCK?” I heard Gertrude yell at the same time Jo let out a scream of rage. There on the surgical table was a giobhioni male, probably a bit tall for their kind, built fairly muscularly, and with their torso slit open from clavicle to pelvis. Various tubes were hooked up to places where internal organs should have been but were missing. At a glance, I could see that besides the lung I’d watched them remove, it looked like his heart was also missing, as well as liver, at least one kidney, and I wasn’t quite sure, but I couldn't see his spleen either. But the remaining lung was still doing its job, and I had to guess so were the other remaining organs. For a second, my brain stopped working. The horror of what I was seeing here, I couldn’t process it. That was a living being, a sentient person, being disassembled piece by piece. “Stop!” I startled out of my shock, my head whipping sideways to see Giselle and Gertrude trying to restrain Jo. She had the kaon lance out, and I could only guess she’d been about to fire on the glass. “They’re still alive, but if you fire that thing in here, they won’t be!” Gertrude was yelling, “And neither will we! There’s gotta be a way down there, we’ll stop them!” “Let me go!” Jo screamed, fighting hard against the twins, causing all three of them to stagger around the room in some kind of violent dance. “Damn it!” I growled out, and looked around desperately for any sign of an interface port. “Stacy! Do you see…” “There!” Stacy cut me off before I could finish, and a marker appeared in my vision, highlighting a spot on the conference table near one of the ends. I ran over to it quickly, but when I got there, all I could see was smooth polished wood. “Stacy…” “It’s underneath! Hurry! If I can get inside, I might be able to get control of those arms!” Pushing the chair out of the way, I fell to my knees and searched the underside of the table. Sure enough, there was an interface port halfway hidden behind a sliding panel. I grabbed the extensible data cable connected to the holo projector in the left forearm of my suit, and tried to connect it to the port. Giobhioni epithets burst out of my mouth as I realized the access port in the table wasn’t a standard type. “Controy! You don’t happen to have a port adapter for this fucking thing, do you?” The captain had been standing at the observation window, frozen at the horror he was watching, and I growled in frustration. I reached out and grabbed the chair I’d pushed away, and launched it in his direction, hoping to snap him out of it. I might have launched it a bit too hard. It wheeled across the space between us and hit him squarely in the back, causing him to stumble forward and smack his face on the glass. For a second I worried I’d just ended up stunning him even more, but he pushed himself away from the glass, turned around and saw the chaos. “Fucking hells, Commander! Don’t!” “Captain!” I yelled over the sounds of the women in the room screaming at each other, “Port adapter! Do you have one?!” He glanced at me, blinked, then hurriedly dug in his pockets. Moments later and an underhand toss, and I had the cable slotted into the table. I could feel Stacy slice into the network, firewalls and encryption being brutally flayed with bleeding edge urgency. I saw intrusion countermeasures reach out and attempt a counter offence against her. Instinctively, I found myself reaching out and grabbing duplicates or the digital weapons Stacy was using, along with bizarre codes that manifested from the Keeper archive, and corral the countermeasure constructs in digital razorwire, visualizing it spinning around like a demented chainsaw, slicing lines of code off the constructs as they continued to struggle towards Stacy. It felt like I held on the blasted things for hours before Stacy reached out and grabbed control from me. I’ve got it Babykins! She called out, Go stop the commander! Satisfied that Stacy would take action in the operating theater, I turned and hurried over to where the twins were trying to keep Jo from getting a clear shot with the kaon lance. You would think the two well muscled blondes wouldn't have had much of an issue overwhelming the diminutive Jophixa, but giobhioni had a muscle density that outmatched us humans. I knew from personal experience that Jo could easily overpower me. And while I wasn't any kind of heavy or muscular, I was certainly Twice her size. Gertrude and Giselle were being spun around as if they were on some sort of demented theme park ride, instead of holding on to a 125 centimeters or pissed off alien. The still held on, however, and we're effectively keeping her from Firing her lance. When I got close to them, I hesitated. I wasn't any sort of melee combatant, and jumping into the fray was as likely to cause Jo to break free than to over power her. I drew a blank on how I was going to do anything useful. Finally, grinding my teeth, I decided to just try the simplest thing. I yelled at her. “Jophixa!” I bellowed at the loudest I could manage, “I got Stacy into their system! She's taking control! She'll stop them! Stop! Please!” She didn't hear me at first, having gone almost completely feral at the sight of one of her people being butchered alive. I couldn't blame her, knowing that as far as she knew, there were so few of them left. But I had to get through to her, so I just kept yelling the same thing to her. Then, in a booming voice that rattled the walls, Stacy came over the public address system with such a fierce tone of furious command that it shocked all of us into a statue-like stillness. “You will cease this criminal butchery!” She commanded, “And you will put this poor soul back together, or I will tear this one apart, piece by piece! I will then move to the next one, and the next, until you comply. “If you refuse, I will make certain every last human involved in this tragedy dies a horrible, tortuous death, and then I will track down every single person you love…”

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Volume 4 - Chapter 11

She wasn't one to threaten organics lightly. She had made that a personal rule shortly after meeting Thomas, especially after perusing some of the popular media his people created, with their paranoia about AI. She didn't want anyone to decide she was some kind of inherent danger to them. it was one thing when the commander ordered her to take action, or when she was acting to protect her crew, especially her beloved Tomminoms. She would certainly like nothing more than to see Benson Fisch or the ktonshi blasted into atoms, but they had earned that ire. For anyone else, she preferred to give them a chance in altering their course of actions. So when she was able to infiltrate the Parthax outposts computer system, and take over the surgical robots, she didn't even think about simply killing the monstrous humans performing the vivisection. She merely grabbed the one the records said was the most senior, and lifted him into the air by both arms. “ You will put that poor giobhioni back together,” she repeated, “or I will perform the same atrocities on this one. I will then move to another of you.” To drive her point home, she began pulling the man's arms in opposite directions, causing him to scream in pain. There was a flurry of desperate activity as the surgical staff got to immediate work, attempting to reimplant all the internal organs they'd removed. If she'd had a mouth to smile with, she would have been grinning at their efforts to comply. Overall, she would have much preferred to get Tzaki Tratsa here to do the work, or better yet, gotten the poor bugger to the Elegance for the work to be done. but after the violent altercations on the way, she didn't trust Tratsa would be safe. Please tell the commander I've got it under control. She whispered through Thomas' implant. If they don't fix this guy up, they will regret it. I'm also searching their database to see if they have any more giobhioni here. Thanks Digibits, he sent in response, she's starting to calm down now. Is there a way down there from where we are? There is a lift on the far wall from where you are. she told him, it opens in some kind of waiting room. I'll send it up to you. Thanks! Might help calm Jo down if she knows she can access those monsters if that poor bastard doesn't make it. The man she was holding - one Doctor Philleas Montblanc - started making sounds of distress, and she guessed that holding him a meter off the floor by his outstretched arms was likely doing damage to his anatomy. While she couldn't care less about any pain he suffered, no after observing what they had done, she didn't want to risk that his cries would distract the other doctors in the room. She wanted the best chances that the poor soul they mutilated would recover. She took her original task as the caretaker for the giobhioni on the station seriously, and finding even one other alive after all this time would increase morale. She gently lowered Montblanc so his feet were on the floor, but continued restraining him with his arms stretched out sideways. If any of them tried something stupid, the kstansi would pay for it, and if their patient died, so would the rest. In the meantime, she was diving deep into the systems she had access to now, copying every scrap of data into isolated storage back on the station. She dedicated massive amounts of her core processors to that, and searching for information on why they were doing this, if there were any more giobhioni here, and what records might exist of what happened to her sweet, sweet honey bunch engineers mother. There was a lot of data to pour through, as if Benson Fisch had been using this site as a secure, dead drop data storage facility for quite some time. There were thousands of zettabytes of information archived here, from astronautics research to xenobiology. Even for her, it was going to take time to sort through given the encryption levels. Hours later, as the surgeons seemed to be ready to drop from exhaustion, but work to restore the patient was almost completed, she came upon a file buried deep in an obscure file tree. It looked like nothing more than a garbage file at first, but she analyzed it anyway. Oh, she found herself saying in the deep virtual universe of the server, Oh no no no! Poor Thomas! He will be devastated! She found herself thinking that maybe it was time to take a direct hand in making sure that Benson Fisch never contributed to the darkness of the galaxy ever again. The lift let us out into something of a waiting room, with comfortable armchairs and couches. Holomonitors were mounted along one wall, flanking a heavy duty airlock hatchway that I guessed led into the operating room. On the wall to the right was a hot beverage dispenser, a soda fridge, and a vending machine full of junk food. The left wall was covered in flat panel info boards, displaying what appeared to be rules and regulations to do with the use of the surgical suite. Most of them were about the classified nature of the work, proper decontamination practices for both preceding entry into, and exit from the operating room. There were some serious penalties listed for infractions, including that of expulsion to the surface of the moon for breach of secrecy. whoever in the chain of command was in bed with Fisch about what was going on here, seriously needed to see the inside of a prison. I know the Commonwealth had laws against performing vivisections on sophont species, and those laws were part of treaties signed with our interstellar neighbors. If this got out to, for example, the Benastians, it could cause regional tensions we hadn't seen in over a century. Orion's sword! I hope that giobhioni survives, and they haven't done this to others before this. I thought to myself, sitting on one of the couches and keeping hold of one of Jo's hands while we waited for word from Stacy. I don't even want to know how things will play out if… Good news and bad news Tomminoms. We won't know if he will survive or not yet, but Tratsa believes he will. Or at least, he should live long enough for us to transport him to Elegance so she can take over his care. Once that happens, she can virtually assure his recovery.” I breathed a sigh of relief, echoed by both the twins and Controy. They'd probably been having similar thoughts to mine. I glanced sideways at Jo to see her shoulders relax in a slump, but I could still feel a certain amount of tension in her hand. “Thank you Stacy.” she said quietly, “Were you able to find out who he is, or anything?” - “So far their records only list him as GS-12.” Stacy informed her. “They…they found twenty five of our people in stasis pods. There's only fourteen left alive.” We all turned to glare hard at the airlock leading to the surgical suite. Eleven giobhioni lives had been ended here, experimented on, cut open and parted out like some derelict ship, instead of the sophont beings they were. I could taste bile in the back of my throat and had to force myself to swallow it down. “Stacy, please relay this information to the admiral,” Jo said coldly, her shoulders once again tensed up, she looked like she really wanted to go feed those monsters into a reactor core and watch them slowly dissolve from the heat and radiation. Not that I could blame her. “This isn't going to stand. I want every human that didn't arrive with me out of this outpost.” This story originates from a different website. Ensure the author gets the support they deserve by reading it there. “Sepaq, commander.” Stacy replied, “Notifying the admiral now. I will also include a demand that everyone involved in this horror show be up on charges; if that meets your approval, ma'am.” She nodded, then turned to the captain. “I loathe to put off seeing these stasis pods, not to mention waking them,” Jo told him, her eyes violet eyes hard, “but it's obvious we cannot trust any of your people to escort that poor giobhioni back to Elegance. We'll head back together.” Controy tilted his head in acknowledgment.. “Can't blame you Commander. I'd do the same in your shoes.” he sighed, “Can’t say I’m happy to lose the posting though.” “I'm sure the admiral will put in a good word for you, captain.” Gertrude said with a smile. We waited another half an hour in silence for the medical telemetry to show the giobhioni man to be stable enough for transport. When Stacy gave the go ahead, we headed into the operating theatre to retrieve him. Gertrude, Jo and Controy filed in first with weapons at the ready, in case the surgical team tried anything, but Stacy still had one of them pinned as if to an invisible cross. The rest of them looked terrified that the robotic arms she was using would do something nightmarish, like rip him in two and use the parts as cudgels. Now that they weren't occupied with putting the poor bastard back together, they were huddled in a corner, looking as if they were seriously questioning their life choices. One of them, a middle aged woman with badly wrinkled skin, stepped forward and attempted to say something, but the look Jophixa leveled at her made her wilt back into the corner. Giselle and I grabbed hold of the hospital gurney holding the unconscious giobhioni, and started wheeling it towards the doors. The others closed in behind us, with Jo moving in front to make sure we weren't caught by an ambush in the waiting room. Luckily, we encountered no more trouble on the way back to the ship. There were a few tense moments, when we passed security personnel in the corridors who scowled at us as we went by. Some of them had a tension about them that made the hairs on the back of my neck stand on end. If it hadn't been for our armed trio looking hair-triggered and ready to ventilate anyone who so much as sneezed wrong… well, I guessed the trip would have been far more problematic. When we got back to the hangar, Tratsa met us at the loading ramp, with Tindron and Boudya, armed with Ppg rifles, flanking her to ensure the base's personnel didn't try anything. It was honestly a scene to remind me that despite the benastian reputation for being hedonists, with their long, lithe bodies and unconscious grace and sensuality, they also tended to be intimidating warriors as well. Only a fool wrote off a benastian as a harmless sexual degenerate, as it generally ended with said fool missing body parts. Tratsa quickly took charge of our mystery patient, getting him to Med-bay without delay, and immediately got him hooked up to various medical devices. She then gave me a quick peck on the cheek and admonished me to be careful, and to keep my helmet on. “You've had enough blows to the head, anymore and I'll need to start tracking your mental abilities for decline.” she growled at me, and smacked my ass when I turned to leave. When we started back towards the research wing of the outpost, Tindron joined us at Jo's request, decked out in his own lightweight armor, with the PPG rifle slung over his shoulder. His presence with us made me feel a bit safer. Don't get me wrong, Jo and the twins proved they were dependable in a firefight, but there was something about having two and a quarter meters of blue alien elf, garbed in stealthy black armor, helmet giving the impression of a vicious alien jaguar, that made me feel far more backed up. “I wonder if that guy was the source of the omega band signal.” he asked as we started back down the corridor that led to the surgical theater. “Tratsa has checked his implant,” Stacy put in, once again speaking through my suit. “definitely not the source. His name is Lieutenant Drunti Seraoh, of Giobhioni Forces special project division. Omega channel transponders are only assigned to command rank personnel.” Special project division huh? I thought to myself. I wonder what kinds of projects count as -special-. This outpost would be something that fell under their bailiwick, she explained, without the monstrous vivasectioning of sophont species that is. Weapons research, classified starship design and development. Heck, contagion research would have been part of their purview, trying to understand it and develop protections and cures. Hey! she exclaimed, If they were still operating, I bet they'd start researching what it is that makes you such an irresistible bowl of vixtaerian pudding. I stumbled slightly when she said it, and tried to act nonchalant when the group turned to look at me. I had mostly gotten used to Stacy's outrageous flirting, especially since she had a habit of doing it directly through my implant now, so Jo couldn't give her shit for “unprofessional conduct during a sensitive situation”. But every so often, she still caught me off guard. Thankfully, nobody commented on my faltered step, and we continued on back to the secure wing of the outpost. “The other subjects…” The look of absolute, murderous intent that was directed at the medical researcher by multiple people seemed to shake her to the very core and she swallowed audibly before starting again, “The other giobhioni are through there.” she informed us, pointing to another secure door. Her directions weren't exactly required now that Stacy had control of the secure mainframe, having found a means to bridge it over to Elegance as well. But Controy had immediately demanded one of their group ‘spill the beans’ as they said in the old flatvids. Stacy figured it was his way of seeing if they'd attempt to pull a fast one, maybe direct us to a guard station or something. Sure enough, the first to answer had attempted to do just that, but this woman - a doctor named Emelia Lang - had shouted him down and informed us of the duplicity. With Stacy already knowing the truth, and confirming it for us, the captain hauled off and pistol whipped the original speaker, knocking him out cold. “Are there any guards stationed there?” Gertrude demanded of the woman, “or along the way?” “There are usually two positioned in the stasis bay itself.” Lang quickly reported, wincing at the ferocity of the question. “Not usually in the corridor. But if anyone was monitoring security feeds, it's possible that security has deployed more guards.” Exchanging glances with Jo and Tindron, I had a feeling we were all thinking the same thing. We could always hedge our bets in the event more guards had been deployed by using these monstrous wastes of oxygen as shields. push them ahead of us through the door, so that the guards would have to shoot through them to get to us. It would certainly get a little back for their inhuman acts against another sophont species. I raised an eyebrow at Jo and waited for her orders. She was the commander after all. “They haven't deployed more security.” Stacy said, likely picking up on my thoughts. “Well, that's not quite true. they have, but not to the stasis chamber. Now that I'm in the system, I managed to divert orders. They've been redirected to some sort of warehouse facility, they… “Oh my,” she continued, “We'll want to secure that warehouse Commander. There is at least one giobhioni ship stored there.” “Thank you, Stacy.” Jo replied with a quick nod, “that will make this easier. Dragging the lot of you with us would have been a pain in the gums. One of you would likely try something stupid and force me to rip your spleen out. I'd rather leave you to LeBeau to sort out. “You though,” and she turned to Lang, “I think we will take you with us. Behave and I might even tell the Admiral you cooperated. Might buy you some leniency.” Again, the woman audibly gulped like something out of an old looney tune, but she nodded, and carefully made her way to the door she'd pointed out earlier. “You're friend Stacy probably knows already, but there's a lockdown protocol for the O.R. She can lock the rest up in there.” “Too right,” Stacy confirmed, “especially since I'll kill all access to any terminals in the room. They won't be able to hack their way out.” Dr. Lang blinked, then looked crestfallen. She was probably hoping they could do just that and escape. I thought to myself as I fell into step behind Jo. Fat chance of that snookums. Stacy replied, her voice tinged with dark humor. The commander is going to want all of them to suffer consequences for what they’ve done. As far as we know, they’ve murdered eleven giobhioni in their so-called research. She wasn’t wrong, and I was in full agreement. I wasn’t a big fan of capital punishment - there were too many cases in history of wrongful convictions - but this was too much to let slide. Jo was being remarkably controlled about the whole thing, considering how few of her kind were left. Eleven lives might not seem like much, but every gram of genetic diversity was important when a species could be numbered in the thousands. Whatever came next, the people here needed to pay.

▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓
  19. The Eye and The Serpent
  Tác giả: Calin Zenn
  Tổng chương: 7
  Ngày cập nhật: N/A
  URL: https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/158751/the-eye-and-the-serpent
▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓

  Mô tả: In 2121, a woman is killing the world. Not with war. Not with weapons. With hunger. With madness. With wind spells so precise they only leave one in a thousand people standing — and she has a reason for every single survivor. Sekh is a destroyer, sent across the galaxy to stop her. She's eliminated chaos on a hundred worlds. This should be no different. The woman knows her name. Knows her training. Knows the shape of her meditation chamber on a world Sekh has never spoken of to anyone. She has been talking to her reflection for centuries, waiting for the other half of herself to finally arrive. They are twins. Separated at birth. Raised as opposites. And the chaos-bringer didn't just lure Sekh to Earth — she needs something only Sekh carries. Set in 2121, The Eye and The Serpent blends Egyptian mythology, dark humor, and cosmic stakes into a story about two women who are the most dangerous things in the universe — separately. Together is another question entirely.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  CHAPTER FIVE: DAY TWO
  https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/158751/the-eye-and-the-serpent/chapter/3200208/chapter-five-day-two
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Dawn came with a chime from the pod’s AI. “Good morning, valued guest. Current time: 6:00 AM. Checkout is at 10:00 AM. Weather today: partly cloudy, high of 89 degrees. Would you like to extend your stay?” Sekh opened her eyes. She hadn’t slept—didn’t need to—but she’d rested. Let her body complete the work it had started in the crash site. The ribs were fully knit now, the shoulder muscle repaired, the deep-tissue bruising resolved overnight into nothing. Her physical form was whole. That was the easy part. Her reserves were another matter. She took stock in the careful way she’d been trained to—not with alarm, because alarm was not a useful response to information, but with honesty. The vehicle push. The employee at Fashion Nexus. Brick. The diner wipe. Ella. Five significant draws in less than twenty-four hours, on top of the physical trauma of the crash and the power she’d spent sealing the crater. She was not depleted. But she was running at something closer to two-thirds than she would have liked, heading into a confrontation with a chaos-bringer whose strength she still couldn’t accurately measure from a distance. She would need to be careful today. Conservative. She had never been good at conservative. “No extension needed,” she said. “Thank you for choosing Comfort Pods. We hope you enjoyed your stay. Please remember to take all personal belongings.” She had no belongings. The rolled Nref bodysuit in her jacket pocket didn’t count—it barely registered as mass. She checked it anyway, a small automatic gesture, the kind of thing you do when you want to confirm that at least one thing is exactly where you left it. It was. She stepped outside. The rest area was already busy. Morning commuters stopping for coffee-substitute and breakfast bars, their implants flickering as they processed the day’s first data feeds. A family loading into their vehicle with the particular controlled chaos of people who had done this before and had stopped pretending it would go smoothly. Two small children argued about something in the back seat while a parent loaded bags with the focused patience of someone who had long since stopped expecting help. And a group of cyclists, off to one side, preparing for their day’s journey. Actual bicycles. Human-powered, chain-driven, entirely analogue in a world that had moved past the need for them. They wore their anachronism like a statement. One of them had a flag attached to the back of her seat—not a cause or a brand but a hand-painted image, a snake eating its own tail, circling endlessly. Snake Year. Even the cyclists. Sekh watched them for a moment before joining the pedestrian path. She had developed, over the past two days, a particular appreciation for the cyclists. Something about their insistence on moving through the world by their own effort, in a manner their bodies had evolved for, struck her as both pointless and admirable. She couldn’t have explained why. Thirty-four miles to Wynnewood. She started walking. The landscape changed as the morning wore on. Less agricultural. More wild. The engineered flatness of cultivated land giving way to something older, something that hadn’t been reshaped to human specifications and showed no interest in being. Trees clustered thick along ridgelines, their canopies dense enough to throw real shadow across the road. The air was heavier here—more humid, carrying the smell of water and decomposing leaves and the particular richness of soil that hadn’t been turned in a long time. Oklahoma, she was understanding, was a place that had been many things and was now being left to decide what it wanted to be next. The infrastructure was there—roads, power lines, the occasional charging hub—but worn at the edges. The kind of worn that happened when maintenance budgets were redirected somewhere else for long enough that everyone stopped noticing the deterioration. She passed through three towns in two hours. The first had a gas station, a post office, and a diner with a hand-painted sign offering something called “actual coffee.” Four people sat at the outdoor tables. They watched Sekh pass with the unhurried attention of people who had nowhere pressing to be. The second town had no open businesses that she could see. Buildings with faded paint and windows that might have been dark for years or might have been dark since last week—it was impossible to tell. A man sat on a porch and lifted a hand as she passed. She lifted one back. That was the entirety of their interaction, and it felt, somehow, like enough. The third town had a school. Small, clearly repurposed from something older, with a playground that mixed the equipment of several different decades. A group of children were chasing each other across it with the absolute commitment of people for whom this was the most important thing happening in the world. One of them stopped and stared at Sekh as she passed on the road below. Sekh looked back. The child waved. Sekh waved. The child screamed something to its companions and resumed running. This was flyover country. Forgotten America. The places people left, not the places they came to. Perfect hiding place for someone who didn’t want to be noticed. Or perfect hiding place for something that had already noticed everything. That thought arrived unbidden and she let it sit. Around mid-morning, the Washita River appeared. She smelled it before she saw it—that particular smell of moving water that had been carrying Oklahoma clay for long enough that the water and the earth had become inseparable. When the tree line broke and the river came into view, she stopped walking. It was not a beautiful river in the way that some rivers were beautiful—not clear, not quick, not dramatic with rock faces or white water. The Washita was slow and brown and opaque, its surface the color of old terra cotta, moving with the unhurried confidence of something that had been doing this for longer than anything watching it had existed. Its banks were red clay, dense and crumbling at the edges where the water had worked at them. The trees along the banks were heavy with summer, their roots visible where the river had undercut the soil, reaching into the water like fingers unwilling to let go. Muddy. Ancient. Indifferent. Something about it reached her in a way she hadn’t expected. On Nref, water was managed. Contained. Directed through engineered channels to serve specific functions. It did not have character. It did not look like it had been somewhere. This river looked like it had been everywhere. She stood on the bank for a moment longer than was necessary, watching the brown water move. Then she crossed at a shallow point, the clay bottom solid beneath her feet, and continued south. This content has been misappropriated from Royal Road; report any instances of this story if found elsewhere. Wynnewood was close enough now that she could feel it. Not just the chaos-scent—that had been with her for days, growing steadily, pulling steadily. Something else. That second thing. The one she’d been calling a feeling because she didn’t have a better word for it, and refusing to give it a better word felt, in some irrational way, like refusing to give it power. It was stronger on this side of the river. She noted this and walked faster. Around noon, Sekh encountered her first real checkpoint. It appeared around a bend in the highway—a barrier across the road, two official vehicles parked on either side, four people in uniforms bearing the logo of what her translator identified as a private festival security contractor. A sign proclaimed: FESTIVAL CHECKPOINT — ALL TRAVELERS MUST VERIFY CREDENTIALS. Sekh slowed. Assessed. The checkpoint was thorough. Each vehicle stopping, each occupant scanning their implant, verifying identity, proving they had legitimate reason to travel to Wynnewood during the festival. The line of vehicles waiting to pass stretched back around the bend. The officers were unhurried. Professional. The kind of professional that came from a company that took contracts seriously. She had no implant. No credentials. No identity in any system this checkpoint could access. She could turn back. Find another route. Circle around through the wilderness—she’d crossed enough flooded fields in the last two days that a few miles of off-road travel held no particular difficulty. Or she could walk straight through. Four minds simultaneously. She’d done it yesterday at the diner and noted the cost. Her reserves were not what they’d been. The smart choice was the long way around. She kept walking toward the checkpoint. Some choices were not really about what was smart. As she approached, one of the officers held up a hand. “Ma’am? We’re going to need to verify your credentials before you continue.” Sekh locked eyes with him. Pushed her thought into his mind and the three others simultaneously: I have already been verified. I passed through ten minutes ago. You remember waving me through. Three of them blinked. Nodded. Waved her on. The fourth—a woman with sharp eyes and an implant that glowed distinctly brighter than the others, the particular brightness of high-end hardware—frowned. “Wait. I don’t remember—” Sekh pushed harder. Yes, you do. You checked her yourself. Brown jacket, dark pants. Told her to enjoy the festival. The woman’s frown deepened. Her hand moved toward the weapon at her hip with the practiced ease of someone who had done it enough times that the motion was reflex rather than decision. Sekh stopped walking. Met her eyes directly. For a long moment they were locked—Sekh’s power pressing against the woman’s implant-reinforced cognition, the woman’s trained mind pushing back with the particular stubbornness of someone who had been told, professionally, to trust what she remembered. The implant flickered. Went dark for half a second. And when it came back online— “Right,” the woman said slowly. “Sorry. Brain fart. Too much coffee. You’re good to go.” Sekh smiled. “Thank you, officer.” She walked past the checkpoint and didn’t look back. But she felt it. The draw. More than yesterday—she’d had to push harder on the resistant one, had to hold all four longer than she’d intended. Her reserves, which she’d tallied at roughly two-thirds this morning, had dropped. She was running below half now. Below half, heading into whatever was waiting at the end of this road. She filed this under problems she would solve when they became problems, and continued walking. But she also filed one other thing: the implant. She’d manipulated it directly, not just the mind behind it. Reached into the hardware and dimmed it long enough for the mental rewrite to take hold. That was new. She hadn’t known she could do that until she’d done it. Good to know. The final stretch to Wynnewood was different. More people. All moving the same direction, all carrying the particular energy of people heading somewhere they’d been looking forward to. Vehicles filled with festival-goers, their windows down, music escaping in fragments that her translator couldn’t quite keep up with. Pedestrians in clothing that had made deliberate choices about color. Cyclists with banners. A group walking in matching shirts that read SNAKE YEAR OR BUST in letters that suggested someone had found this very funny. The Snake Year celebration. The woman she’d come to find had timed this perfectly. Maximum population density. Maximum movement. Maximum confusion. A thousand strangers flooding a dying town, and somewhere in the middle of it, one woman who had been waiting for exactly this crowd. Clever. Sekh walked through the crowd without touching anyone, a habit so automatic she barely noticed it anymore. Her presence moved people slightly—they stepped aside without knowing why, conversation pausing as she passed and resuming when she’d gone, like water parting around something submerged. And then, somewhere in the last five miles, the feeling changed. It had been with her since the Transit Hub—that low persistent rhythm, like a second heartbeat out of sync with her own. She’d noticed it. Carried it. Filed it, unfiled it, told herself it was a system artifact from the crash, a harmonic of buried technology, anything except what some part of her had known it was since the first time it reached her. Now it wasn’t low and persistent. Now it was loud. Not a heartbeat. A pulse. Steady and deliberate and unmistakably directed—not emanating from the chaos-scent she’d been following for two days, but alongside it. Separate from it. Something that knew her frequency and was broadcasting on it with the calm confidence of something that had been doing so for a very long time and was simply waiting for her to stop pretending she couldn’t hear it. Sekh’s steps faltered. The crowd moved around her, oblivious. Familiar. The feeling was familiar in the most impossible sense of the word—not familiar like a place she’d been, not familiar like a person she’d met. Familiar like her own reflection. Like her own voice speaking from a room she was certain was empty. She’d been told she was singular. Unique. The only one of her kind—a weapon forged alone, shaped alone, sent forth alone. She had never questioned this. On Nref, there was nothing that pressed you toward questions, and singularity had always felt less like a fact about her and more like the natural shape of things. Of course there was only one. There was only ever one. The feeling didn’t care what she’d been told. She stopped in the middle of the pedestrian path. A cyclist swerved around her with a curse she didn’t register. The crowd parted and closed and parted again. She stood still in the center of it and let the feeling arrive fully, the way you let a wave arrive when you’ve already decided not to run from it. Recognition. Someone ahead knew she was coming. Had known she was coming. Had been looking in mirrors for years—decades, centuries maybe—talking to a reflection that lived on another world. And that reflection had finally, finally gotten on a ship and come. The chaos-bringer. Her— No. Sekh forced herself to keep moving. The mission was the mission. The purpose was the purpose. She had a target and a directive and five hundred years of training that had never once suggested she stop in the middle of a crowd to have a feeling. But the word was already there. Had probably been there since she first picked up the scent in her meditation chamber, buried under everything else she’d decided was more important. Twin. “No,” Sekh said, quietly, to no one in particular. A woman passing in the opposite direction gave her a sideways look and kept walking. The universe offered no response. It had never been especially interested in what she found acceptable. She started walking again. Faster now, as if speed could settle the question. The final miles moved beneath her feet with a momentum that felt less like her own and more like something she’d been pulled toward since before she’d left Nref. Since before the scent had reached her. Maybe since before she’d ever sat in a meditation chamber and told herself she didn’t feel anything. The two things she’d been carrying since the crash surfaced one last time, quiet and persistent: the Council’s voice, checking on her productivity before her ribs had finished knitting. The signal, galaxy-detectable from a town of two thousand. She turned them over briefly in the back of her mind. She was beginning to think those two things were connected. Was beginning to think everything was connected. Was beginning to think she had been aimed at this place with considerably more precision than she’d been told. She pushed it all down. There would be answers when she arrived. The sun had begun its descent toward evening when the lights of Wynnewood appeared on the horizon. Small. Unremarkable. The kind of town that existed in the spaces between other things, that showed up on maps only because someone had decided the map should be thorough. Its lights were modest—a cluster of them, gold and warm against the darkening sky, festival decorations adding color that the town itself probably hadn’t seen in decades. From this distance, it looked like nothing. It felt like everything. Sekh walked toward it, her power at half, her reserves drawn down by two days of careful expenditure on a planet that had surprised her at every turn. The twin-feeling was so loud now she’d stopped trying to categorize it. It was simply there. Part of the air. Part of the rhythm of her steps. Something was waiting for her in that town. Something that had been waiting for a very long time. She stopped fighting the knowledge and let it settle into certainty, the way all honest things eventually settle when you stop arguing with them. And waited for whatever came next.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  CHAPTER SIX: WYNNEWOOD
  https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/158751/the-eye-and-the-serpent/chapter/3203932/chapter-six-wynnewood
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Wynnewood wasn’t a town. It was a graveyard pretending to be alive. Sekh entered from the north as the sun painted everything gold. Main Street—the only real street—was lined with buildings that had seen better days in a way that suggested they’d stopped expecting them to return. A hardware store with windows covered in a decade of dust. A diner that might have been open, might have been abandoned—the sign was lit but the parking lot was empty, and she couldn’t tell if that was a business operating on hope or a light someone had forgotten to turn off. A gas station converted into a charging hub for electric vehicles, its original branding still visible beneath the new signage like a palimpsest. And everywhere, festival decorations. Banners in red and gold—Snake Year colors—strung between light poles and storefronts with the slightly irregular tension of things hung by volunteers rather than professionals. Vendor stalls being assembled in empty lots, their operators working with the focused efficiency of people who had done this in a hundred towns and knew exactly how much time they had. Stages erected in parking lots that had probably never held this many people. And the people themselves—so many of them, more than this town had probably seen in decades, filling the streets like blood rushing back into a limb that had been numb so long it had forgotten what circulation felt like. Temporary. Desperate. Bright. Wynnewood had no hotels. The historic Eskridge Hotel on Main Street—once the jewel of the county, a three-story brick building that had hosted traveling salesmen and local celebrations and all the quiet dramas of a town that used to matter—had long since been converted to a museum. It stood at the far end of the street with its original facade intact and its front doors locked, a monument maintained by people who understood that what you preserve says as much about you as what you build. The festival organizers had solved the accommodation problem in the way modern festivals solved everything: money and technology. Glamping yurts had been installed in clusters along the river road—smart-glass roundhouses with adaptive climate systems and premium bedding, imported and erected for the week at a cost that would have repaved every cracked road in Garvin County twice over. They cost more per night than most Wynnewood residents earned in a month. The town received a percentage of every booking. It was not enough to fix anything. But in a town that most people passed through without slowing down, not enough was still something. Sekh moved through the crowd like a ghost. Not invisibly—she had stopped spending power on active concealment hours ago, conserving what remained for whatever came next. She moved through it the way she had always moved through crowds: present but untouchable, her body occupying space without inviting contact. People stepped aside without knowing why. Conversations paused as she passed and resumed when she was gone. A child pointed. A vendor glanced up from their setup and then looked quickly away. Her senses were loud now. The chaos-scent—which had been a beacon for two days, steady and directional, pulling her southeast with the patient insistence of a compass—was close enough that she could feel its texture without reaching for it. It was here. In this town. Concentrated somewhere ahead, past the vendor stalls and the stage lights and the festival-goers who had no idea what they were celebrating in proximity to. And underneath it, that other thing. The one she’d stopped arguing with somewhere in the last five miles of road. Twin. She let herself feel it fully for the first time. Let it sit in her awareness without the reflexive urge to categorize it as something else. It was what it was. It had always been what it was. And it was pulling her forward with a certainty that made the chaos-scent feel almost beside the point. She followed both. Past the main street. Past the vendors and stages and the smell of food being prepared in quantities designed for crowds. Past the outer edge of the festival where the decorations thinned and the lighting became ordinary again and the sounds of the crowd softened into something ambient. Into the quieter parts of town where the festival hadn’t reached. Where buildings stood empty with the particular stillness of things that had been empty long enough to stop waiting for occupants. Where streets cracked from years of freeze and thaw and neglect, vegetation pushing through the splits in a slow, patient reclamation. This was what Wynnewood actually was, underneath the festival. She walked through it without looking away. To the river. The Washita moved slow and brown through the landscape—the same river she’d crossed that morning, or a different stretch of the same ancient thing. Muddy in the way Oklahoma rivers were always muddy, its water the color of the red clay it had been carrying since before any human had been alive to name it. The banks were dense and root-tangled, the trees heavy with late spring. Evening light caught the surface and turned it briefly copper before the shadows took it back. This tale has been unlawfully obtained from Royal Road. If you discover it on Amazon, kindly report it. Sekh paused at the bank. She felt them immediately. The microbes—distributed through the entire river system, riding the current, present in every drop of water that touched the bank where she stood. They registered her the same way the flood colony had registered her that morning. The same hesitation. The same retreat. The same reorganization, that not-quite-communication buzzing through the colony like a signal through wire. This time she was less surprised. More troubled. Because the Washita ran through Wynnewood. Had always run through Wynnewood. And if the microbes were here—if the same signal was present in this water that had been present in the floodwater twenty miles north—then whatever had organized them had been here a long time. Had been in this specific river. Had made this specific place its ground. The signal rose from the water around her feet, familiar now, terrible now: Mother. Sekh filed it for later. There was a great deal accumulating in that file. She crossed and kept walking. On the far bank, maybe a quarter mile from the edge of town, the glamping yurts rose against the evening sky in a cluster—smart-glass roundhouses that caught the last of the light and held it differently than ordinary glass would, their adaptive materials shifting subtly as the temperature dropped. Premium accommodations for festival-goers who wanted proximity to the experience without the inconvenience of actually roughing it. The operators moved between them with the quiet efficiency of hospitality professionals, and the guests moved between them with the particular self-consciousness of people who had paid enough to feel they deserved to be comfortable. One yurt sat apart from the others. Larger. Set back from the cluster by a distance that felt deliberate. Its windows didn’t catch the light the way the others did—didn’t reflect it, exactly, seemed to absorb it, to take it in and give nothing back. The smart-glass was doing something it hadn’t been designed to do, responding to a will that the manufacturers had definitely not anticipated. The chaos-scent poured from it like smoke from a fire that had been burning for years. Sekh stopped at the edge of the campsite. She took stock, quickly and honestly, the way she’d been trained to before any engagement: what did she have, and what would she need. Her power was below half. Considerably below half, if she was being precise, which she was. Five days of draws and a crash landing had left her operating at a fraction of her full capacity. In any of her previous forty-seven missions, she would have assessed this as insufficient for a direct confrontation and found a way to wait, to recover, to arrive whole. She had a hundred miles of walking that had made waiting feel like an insult to everything she’d learned on the way here. She let her power rise to the surface anyway. Not all of it—there was no “all of it” right now, not in the way there had been before she’d left Nref. But what remained. Every fraction of what she had left, drawn up and present and ready. It felt like less than it should have felt like. She pushed that observation aside. It would be enough or it wouldn’t. She had never walked away from a mission before. She didn’t intend to start in a glamping campsite in Oklahoma. Her heart—which never raced, never faltered, had not been built for racing or faltering—was pounding. Inside that yurt was her target. Her mission. Her purpose. Her twin. She walked forward. The yurt’s door opened before she could knock. A woman stood in the doorway. She was beautiful. Not like Sekh was beautiful—not that too-perfect, uncanny thing that made people step back, that registered as wrong even when they couldn’t name why. A-Pop’s beauty was human. Accessible. Warm in the particular way of something that had learned warmth by watching humans practice it and had become very, very good at the imitation. The kind of beautiful that made people lean in rather than away. That made them trust. Dark skin, a shade lighter than Sekh’s. Hair in natural curls pulled back from her face with a practicality that looked effortless. Average height. Average build. Entirely, completely, deliberately ordinary. Except for the eyes. Those eyes were Sekh’s eyes. Golden-brown with flecks of green, ancient in the way that eyes are ancient when the mind behind them has been watching things for longer than it should have been able to. Knowing in the way that eyes are knowing when they have been waiting for this specific moment and have had a great deal of time to prepare for it. Twin. The word arrived in Sekh’s mind not as a shock but as a confirmation. The last piece of something she’d been assembling for two days clicking into place. “Well,” A-Pop said. Her voice was warm honey with razors underneath, the voice of someone who had learned exactly how much warmth was required to make the razors go unnoticed until they were already at work. “Took you long enough.” Sekh’s fingers flexed. Her power coiled—depleted, reduced, a fraction of what it had been, but present and certain and pointed directly at the woman in the doorway. She could end this. Could try to end this. The question of whether it would work with what she had left was a question she did not let herself finish asking. A-Pop smiled. It reached her eyes in a way that was worse than if it hadn’t. “Come in, sister,” she said. “We have so much to talk about.” Sekh’s body held its coil. Ready. Capable. The math of the next half-second was simple and she knew exactly how it worked. It was her mind that moved first. That inconvenient, treacherous thing. A weapon that doesn’t understand its target is wasted. She’d known this for five hundred years. She’d taught it to others. It was, perhaps, the first true thing she’d ever been taught and the last thing she’d expected to use as a reason to walk into a room instead of burning it. And something else. Something that had no name and no business existing inside a destroyer and had been growing quietly in the space behind her sternum since the Transit Hub in Altus, since the Washita floodwater, since a woman named Ella had looked at her like she was remembering something. That something needed to know. Sekh stepped inside.

  20. Dream-Zero: Born from a nightmare called reality
  Tác giả: Awaken Tee
  Tổng chương: 14
  Ngày cập nhật: N/A
  URL: https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/155791/dream-zero-born-from-a-nightmare-called-reality
▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓▓

  Mô tả: In a world where dreams shape power, those known as Somniarchs manifest reality-warping concepts born from their sleep, visions fueled by desire, fear, and trauma. When a quiet high school student named Rei triggers a catastrophic anomaly without even knowing how, the world takes notice. Branded an unregistered threat, Rei is taken by a secret government division that monitors Somniarch activity. But Rei is different. Unlike others who draw power from dreams, he cannot sleep without reliving a nightmare, one where his sister nearly drowned, his parents died, and a nameless demon first whispered to him. That demon never left. As Rei is pushed toward his limits in a world that classifies people by danger level, he faces a truth far worse than imprisonment: his power does not come from dreams… but from something awake. When survival demands a choice, Rei is offered a pact known as The Covenant, a bond that grants terrifying power at the cost of his mind, his autonomy, and eventually his humanity. Because in this world, dreams don’t save you. They consume you. And some nightmares are never meant to end.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Chapter 13: Fractures in motion
  https://www.royalroad.com/fiction/155791/dream-zero-born-from-a-nightmare-called-reality/chapter/3200215/chapter-13-fractures-in-motion
